<item>
  <title>How I scratched and fixed my Canon EFS 17-55mm.</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2011/03/25/how-i-scratched-and-fixed-my-canon-efs-17-55mm/</link>
  <pubDate>Fri, 25 Mar 2011 23:47:25 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://lazygeckoblog.wordpress.com/?p=5</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Saguaro Lake, Phoenix, Arizona.
My knockoff lens hood (3.95$ on ebay) bumps on the railing and flies off the camera. It lands right on the edge of the deck and for a fraction of a second cant decide which way to go. I hold my breath. It flips over and vanishes into the dark waters.

<a href="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/03/group-1-img_6467_img_6473-7-images_resize.jpg"><img class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-6" title="Saguaro Lake Panorama" src="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/03/group-1-img_6467_img_6473-7-images_resize.jpg" alt="" width="570" height="255" /></a>

A few days later I bump my camera against a rock and due to my clumsiness and the missing lens hood, scratch my beloved Canon EF-S 17-55 f2.8 IS USM real bad.

<a href="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/03/imgp9540.jpg"><img class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-9" title="Scatched Lens" src="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/03/imgp9540.jpg" alt="" width="570" height="466" /></a>

A common trick is to fill the scratch with black ink or charcoal.
This works amazingly well with telephoto lenses,
but on a wide lens a dark blob is very visible.

So what are my options now ?
I can send the camera to canon for repair. Canon wont give a quote over the phone but from talking to people I gathered it would be at least 300$. I decided to fix it myself.

The front element is very easy to take off. The ring with the text just peels off.
Underneath are 3 small screws that hold the front element.

<a href="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/03/imgp9541.jpg"><img class="aligncenter size-medium wp-image-10" title="Screws" src="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/03/imgp9541.jpg?w=300" alt="" width="300" height="225" /></a>

The element has a tapering rail on it. Turning it will change the the distance from the other elements in the lens.This is to calibrate the distance focus meter. The lens WILL FOCUS JUST FINE, regardless of how the element is installed. I actually tried this (had nothing to lose).

Now to get a replacement part.
Most people told me that it is impossible to buy lens or camera parts from Canon.
Most people were wrong.

Canon Parts Center at 732-521-7230 will happily sell you any part you can accurately describe.The Canon EF-S 17-55mm Front element part number is YG2-2273-000. Six days and 147$ later I had the new element in my hands.

<a href="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/03/imgp9538.jpg"><img class="aligncenter size-medium wp-image-7" title="IMGP9538" src="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/03/imgp9538.jpg?w=300" alt="" width="300" height="211" /></a>

Mark the screws position on the existing element, pop it out, clean tons of dust, pop in new element as accurately as possible and replace screws and ring-with-text thingy. That's it. Less than 3 minutes work. Test lens, works perfectly.

<a href="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/03/img_7494_resize.jpg"><img class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-13" title="IMG_7494_resize" src="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/03/img_7494_resize.jpg" alt="" width="570" height="380" /></a>

&nbsp;

*** UPDATE ***

I found that the scratched element, when placed on my 50mm f1.8, is an amazing macro diopter.

<a href="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/03/img_4435_resize.jpg"><img class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-30" title="IMG_4435_resize" src="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/03/img_4435_resize.jpg" alt="" width="510" height="340" /></a>

&nbsp;]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>Automatic bird photoraphy using webcam</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2011/03/26/automatic-bird-photoraphy-using-webcam/</link>
  <pubDate>Sat, 26 Mar 2011 15:37:32 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://lazygeckoblog.wordpress.com/?p=23</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Here is an old setup I made a few years ago. That's my (long dead) Canon 300D, a 550FX Flash, EF 70-200mm F4. A webcam is used to detection motion which then triggers the camera via the USB connection. I always liked Canon for their free camera SDK. I am now working on reviving and updating the software. Will post more info here when I'm done.

&nbsp;

<a href="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/03/sunbird-setup.jpg"><img class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-24" title="Sunbird Setup" src="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/03/sunbird-setup.jpg" alt="" width="510" height="408" /></a>]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>Introducing CamJuice</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2011/08/19/introducing-camjuice/</link>
  <pubDate>Fri, 19 Aug 2011 18:00:49 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://lazygecko.org/?p=36</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[CamJuice v1.0 is ready !

CamJuice is an automatic, motion-triggered, photography application for Canon DSLR cameras.Connect your Canon DSLR camera to a laptop or PC, point it somewhere interesting,and step back. When movement is detected, CamJuice will trigger the camera. CamJuice can usethe LiveView feed directly from your Canon camera, or from a regular webcam.
<img class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-37" style="border-color:initial;border-style:initial;" title="CamJuice v1.0" src="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/08/screencap.png" alt="" width="520" height="205" />
<div>

A few samples of what CamJuice can do :
<table width="100%" border="0">
<tbody>
<tr valign="MIDDLE">
<td><a href="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/08/img_8995.jpg"><img class="aligncenter size-thumbnail wp-image-41" title="IMG_8995" src="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/08/img_8995.jpg?w=150" alt="" width="150" height="107" /></a></td>
<td><a href="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/08/img_8250_resize1.jpg"><img class="aligncenter size-thumbnail wp-image-45" title="Dinner Time" src="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/08/img_8250_resize1.jpg?w=150" alt="" width="150" height="126" /></a></td>
</tr>
<tr valign="MIDDLE">
<td><a href="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/08/crw_04221.jpg"><img class="aligncenter size-thumbnail wp-image-44" title="Sun Bird" src="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/08/crw_04221.jpg?w=150" alt="" width="150" height="125" /></a></td>
<td><a href="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/08/img_8341_resize1.jpg"><img class="aligncenter size-thumbnail wp-image-46" title="Mommy " src="http://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2011/08/img_8341_resize1.jpg?w=150" alt="" width="150" height="103" /></a></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<strong>Download:</strong>
<a href="http://dl.dropbox.com/u/623478/CamJuice.zip">CamJuice v1.0</a>

<strong>Installation:</strong>
Unzip file to any folder, run CamJuice to start.

<strong>Requirements:</strong>
Microsoft .Net 4.0 Framework (<a href="http://www.microsoft.com/download/en/details.aspx?id=17851">link</a>)
Windows 7, Vista or XP
Canon DSLR (with LiveView)

<strong>Fun:</strong>
Send us your CamJuice shots, we would love to see them!
<a href="mailto:LazyGeckoOrg@gmail.com">LazyGeckoOrg@gmail.com</a>

<item>
  <title>The LittleRP - A review and Getting Started guide</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2016/02/03/the-littlerp-a-review-and-getting-started-guide/</link>
  <pubDate>Wed, 03 Feb 2016 13:54:35 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://lazygeckoblog.wordpress.com/?p=86</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[We needed a printer to test <a href="http://nunandnoot.com/collections/all">Nn&amp;Noot</a> designs without having to cast them, so we got a LittleRP 3D printer. It took a while to calibrate and get good results, so I decided to write a review and a "getting started" guide. Here it is.
<h3><strong>What is the LittleRP?</strong></h3>
The <a href="http://littlerp.com/">LittleRP</a>is a kit for building a resin based 3D printer, also called an SLA or DLP printer. It uses a DLP projector (hence the name) to cure special resin that hardens when exposed to UV light.
<p style="text-align:center;"><img class="aligncenter" src="https://cdn.shopify.com/s/files/1/1112/9470/files/IMG_4599_large.jpg?14904906087667659227" alt="" width="218" height="253" />
The LittleRP assembled, without the projector mounted.</p>

<h3><strong>So it's a kit, you say</strong></h3>
Yup, that means you have to put it together. It's not terribly complicated, but it will take you 3-6 hours. There are assembly instructions on LittleRP's website, but they are slightly out of date. There are two sets of instructions, be sure to scan through bothbefore you start. Here is a timelapse of me assembling the kit.

https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7OwR96rDh44
<h3><strong>The projector</strong></h3>
The kit is just half the story here, you will need a projector. A DLP projector, to be specific. LCD projectors won'twork, as they don't put out enough UV light. LittleRP has a list of projector models that are known to work. I tried the Acer H6510BDbut found that I could not get it to focus sharp across the entire build area. These projectors are not designed for this kind of use. Theyhave manufacturing variances that are not noticeable in normal operation but come into light (har har) when you try to focus on an object that is 10cm in front of the lens. The projector also died after 10 hours of use, so I returned it and got an Acer X152H instead.
<h3><strong>The Acer X152H</strong></h3>
Compact, HD and relatively cheap, The X152His a good choice for the LittleRP. However, unlike theH6510BD, the X152H needs to be modified to allow focusing on a close target. Actually, most projectors will need to be modded to work with a DLP printer. The projector lens module needs to be moved forward about 5mm. This sounds scary but actually it's a simple process that took me about 10 minutes to complete. If you want to know how it's done (the process is very similar with all the projectors), I will describethe process (with pictures) in a separate blog entry. Once the projector was modified I was able to get a sharp image acrosstheentirebuild plate.

The LittleRP needs to be modified a bit as well, toaccommodatefor the X152H height. The projector is thicker than most, so the printer needsto be moved up by about 18mm, and you will need to drill new mounting holes for the projector on the base plate. Send an email to LittleRP support and they will send you a template to help with the hole drilling.
<h3><strong>The Build Plate and Printer Operation</strong></h3>
<img class=" aligncenter" src="https://cdn.shopify.com/s/files/1/1112/9470/files/littlerp_large.jpg?14343988205041574614" alt="" />

The projector projects an image, it is reflected off the mirror (angled exactly 90 degrees) and goes up through the vat, through the resin, and onto the build plate. The vat is a transparent Teflon foil held in a bracket, and holds about 100cc of resin. The picture abovewas shot mid-print, when starting the build plate should be pressed right into the vat (more about that later). After each layer is cured, the build plate moves upa few mm to allow resin to flow into the gap, and also to peel the print off the Teflon foil, than moves down (by a few mm minus the layer height). The next layer is cured and the process repeats itself.
<h3><strong>Some numbers andprinter specs</strong></h3>
The build plate of the LittleRP is 40x70mm. If you use an HD projector you can achieve feature size of about 36 microns (0.036mm). That is the size of a single pixel on the build plate. The smallest layer height is about 30 microns. This is very, very small. You can print objects with an incredible level of detail and complexity.

Layer cure time varies greatly with the resin being used, but overall it's not a fast process. Anywhere between 5 and 20 seconds per layer is the norm. An object the size of a ring will take 1-2 hours print. Actually, I should say "an object <strong>the height</strong> of a ring". The projector will always cure a full layer, and the cure time is constant, so it doesn't matter how many objects you are able to cram into the build plate. Printing one ring takes the same time as printing 5, as long as they all fit on the plate. Print time is a function of layer count, and not volume.

<img class="aligncenter" src="https://cdn.shopify.com/s/files/1/1112/9470/files/IMG_5298_grande.jpg?6730958599067110773" alt="" />

Objects rising out of glowing resin. Pretty cool.&nbsp;
<h3><strong>Resins andoperational cost</strong></h3>
There are several resins on the market for DLP printers. Some work better than others, some will work better with your projector &amp; printer. The best way to know which works best is to experiment. I had great success with Vorex Orange by <a href="https://shop.madesolid.com/pages/vorex">MadeSolid</a>. I also tried <a href="http://www.makerjuice.com/">MakerJuice</a>, and while the objects look nicer and come out cleaner, I'm having difficulty getting it to render small features.

Resins sells for about $100/kg. This seems a lot, and it's certainly a lot more than the $20/kg you can expectfrom <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fused_deposition_modeling">FDM</a> printers (plastic filament) but remember that DLP printers are designed and suited for printing small items. 1 kg of resin is enough to printabout 200 rings. Castable resin is more expensive, and can go up to $250/kg.
<p style="text-align:center;"><img class="aligncenter" src="https://cdn.shopify.com/s/files/1/1112/9470/files/IMG_5310_large.jpg?3204357336464800346" alt="" />
A <a href="https://www.youmagine.com/designs/attack-mouse" target="_blank">mouse</a>sitting on a quarter.</p>

<h3><strong>The dark side of resin</strong></h3>
Coming from <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fused_deposition_modeling">FDM</a>3D printing, where a finished print is yanked off the build plate and is ready to be used, working with resin can seem like a huge pain in the ass. That's because it is. Uncuredresin is a skin irritant, whenever handling anything about the printer you should be wearing gloves. You will burn through a lot of gloves. The resin is also very viscous, it's hard to pour without dripping, it gets everywhere and it's hard to clean. A failed print will leave floating bits of cured resin in your vat, which are really hard to fish out. If you leave them in there they can stick to your prints and/or ruin the print completely. Changing resin is also an involved process, requiring cleaning the vat withIsopropyl alcohol.Luckilyyou can leave theresin in the vat most of the time, just remember to close up the printer cover. Some resins will require stirring before use, I use a brush to mix up the pigments in the vat if it sat there overnight or longer.
<p style="text-align:center;"><img class="aligncenter" src="https://cdn.shopify.com/s/files/1/1112/9470/files/IMG_5303_large.jpg?5925301408619343682" alt="" />
<a href="http://www.3dbenchy.com/" target="_blank">3D Benchy</a> and baby. That's a penny under the smaller boat.</p>

<h3><strong>Flexvat</strong></h3>
This is an add-on to the LittleRP but I highly recommend that you get it. It replaces the petri dish with a vat that has a transparent yet flexible bottom. This allows the print to peel off easily when the build plate is moving up, instead of just sticking to the glass and ripping off the build plate.

The downside of the flexvat is that the first few layers will be squished and funky.If those layers are important to your print's geometry, you will need to raise the objectit a bit and print support structure. I will write more about support structureand preparing objects in a later blog post.
<h3><strong>Calibration and first layer woes</strong></h3>
As any 3D printer, the LittleRP needs to be calibrated. These tips and procedures apply to almost any DLP printer. Also, like most 3D printers, printing the first layer is the hardest and most important part.There are several parameters you will need to work on:
<ul>
	<li>Flexvat tightness - The foil needs to be taut just the right amount. It should sound like a drum when you tap on it. Yeah, that's hardly scientific but it's all I got. You'll need to experiment.</li>
	<li>Getting the right cure time for initial layers - If you under-cure it will detach from the build plate. Luckily, with the flexvats over-curing is not a problem. I usually cure the first layer for 1.5 minutes, than 10 layers at 25 seconds each.</li>
	<li>Getting the right cure time for the rest of the layers - Again, experimentation is key. I found that the Vorex Orange needs about 10 seconds per layer, while the MakerJuice needs at least 20.</li>
	<li>Z movement speeds and distances - You might need to change this if you have issues with layers detaching. Start with LittleRP's recommended settings (4mm travel distance at 100mm/s).</li>
	<li>Build plate leveling - It has 3 screws, use them to make sure it's perfectly parallel to the flexvat.</li>
	<li>First layer height - I manually move the build plate down, pushing it right into the vat and extending intothe foil, then using the threaded rod I move it up a bit, until it's still pushing down the foil but only a little bit. You will need to do this several times to get a feel for it.</li>
</ul>
<p style="text-align:center;"><img class="aligncenter" src="https://cdn.shopify.com/s/files/1/1112/9470/files/IMG_5302_grande.jpg?2648952254357265834" alt="" />
Two vases printed in Vorex Orange, total print time about 2 hours.</p>

<h3><strong>Starting a print</strong></h3>
Make sure the manual shutter is inserted before turning on the projector. You don't want the projector logo and splash screen cured into your print. I will make a blog post about how to prepare a 3D model for printing, which software to use and how to execute a print.
<h3><strong>Post Processing</strong></h3>
When the print is done the objects are not ready to be handled, as they are covered and infused with uncured resin. This is my post-processing routine:
<ol>
	<li>Detach objects from build plate using an exacto knife. Don't forget to wear gloves.</li>
	<li>Put prints in a small container withisopropyl alcohol</li>
	<li>Put container in an Ultrasonic Jewelry cleaner for 3 minutes</li>
	<li>Remove items, dry, and put in cure box for 10 to 60 minutes, depending on resin.</li>
	<li>Snap off support structure (more about this in a later post)</li>
</ol>
<p style="text-align:center;"><img class="aligncenter" src="https://cdn.shopify.com/s/files/1/1112/9470/files/82214_1000x1000.jpg?825504978000283102" alt="" width="199" height="199" />
Ultrasonic Jewelry cleaner. Get one with a stainless steel container.</p>

<h3><strong>Cure box</strong></h3>
When the objects come out of the Ultrasonic cleaner printer they still have some uncured resin in them, that will eventually seep out and onto your hands. Post-curing is mandatory. You can place them in sun light for an hour, or you can build a cure box from UV led strips ($20 on ebay). I use a nail polish lamp, can be found in Walmart or amazon. Depending on the resin, prints need to be cured for 10 to 120 minutes. Some special resins need to be post-cured for many hours. Over-curing is not a problem, so feel free to just leave them in there for as long as you want.
<p style="text-align:center;"><img class="aligncenter" src="https://cdn.shopify.com/s/files/1/1112/9470/files/41CWKOkIRBL._SX450.jpg?18302513767173358279" alt="" width="232" height="174" />
Nail polish lamp box</p>

<h3><strong>Conclusions</strong></h3>
DLP 3D printing is a great way to get incredible detail on a budget. For less than $1000 you can have a setup that generates professional quality prints. The LittleRP is an excellent kit if you are willing to invest some time in putting it together and learning how to use it. Their support is excellent and responsive, the documentation on the website is adequate although it does need to be updated and reorganized (as of Jan 2016). The LittleRP price can't be beat, it's one of the cheapest DLP printers on the market.
<p style="text-align:center;"><img class="aligncenter" src="https://cdn.shopify.com/s/files/1/1112/9470/files/IMG_5312_grande.jpg?14336047124319799084" alt="" />
The <a href="https://www.youmagine.com/designs/the-shrine" target="_blank">Shrine</a>, printed in MakerJuice resin.</p>
&nbsp;
<h3><strong>Nn&amp;Noot</strong></h3>
Check out our neat 3D printed sculpture jewelry at <a href="http://NunAndNoot.etsy.com" target="_blank">NunAndNoot.etsy.com</a>

&nbsp;

</item>
<item>
  <title>Modifying the Acer X152H Projector to work with a DLP 3D Printer</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2016/02/03/modifying-the-acer-x152h-projector-to-work-with-a-dlp-3d-printer/</link>
  <pubDate>Wed, 03 Feb 2016 15:37:47 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://lazygeckoblog.wordpress.com/?p=100</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<h3><strong>Why the Acer X152H</strong></h3>
It's relatively cheap, It's 1080p and it's easy to modify for short-focus. Also, it's pretty much the only option (as of Jan 2016) if you want an HD image that is crisp across the whole build plate. There are other projectors that are HD and do not require modifying, but you will get blurry corners or worse. I tried an AcerH6510BD and about 1/4 of the image was unusable and blurry.
<h3><strong>What is this modding all about</strong></h3>
The goal is to get the projector to be able to focus on a target that is very close to the lens. The projector was not designed forthis, but fortunately it's relatively simple to hack it to do so. The downside is that once modded, the projector won't able to focus on distant objects. You won't be able touse it as a normal projector anymore. However, the modification is easily reversible.
<h3><strong>Here we go</strong></h3>
Take a deep breath, you are about to crack open and mess around with a brand new $500 device. It's going to be ok. (or not, and if you mess up it's not my fault and you can't sue me, please don't sue me)

Here is ourpatient:

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-129" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_20160121_182649-custom.jpg" alt="IMG_20160121_182649 (Custom).jpg" width="800" height="533" />

&nbsp;

Remove the focus ring, it just pulls right off. No screws.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-133" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_20160121_183141-custom.jpg" alt="IMG_20160121_183141 (Custom).jpg" width="800" height="619" />

&nbsp;

Open all the screws on the bottom, you will need a long Phillips screwdriver. Warning: they are really hard to unscrew, get a good screwdriver so you don't strip the screws. If you do, you are screwed.

Open the lamp cover, take off some weird plastic transparent thing that's glued there, and pop that whitepower connector out of its resting place. No need to disconnect it, just get it out of that nook.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-141" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_20160121_183256-custom.jpg" alt="IMG_20160121_183256 (Custom).jpg" width="800" height="476" />

&nbsp;

The top plastic cover should come off now, butwill still be connected to the main board with a few wires that have plug-in connectors. Disconnect all the wires and set aside the cover.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-144" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_20160121_183344-custom.jpg" alt="IMG_20160121_183344 (Custom).jpg" width="800" height="592" />

&nbsp;

Projector shouldnow look like this:

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-147" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_20160121_183449-custom.jpg" alt="IMG_20160121_183449 (Custom).jpg" width="800" height="534" />

Our goal is to remove the lens assembly, which I have professionally marked with red in the next picture, and "shim" it. We'll be putting it back where it was, only pushed forward by a small amount. This will change the focus distance of the lens.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-153" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_20160121_183456-custom.jpg" alt="IMG_20160121_183456 (Custom).jpg" width="800" height="547" />

The lens assembly is held in place with fourM3 bolts. The bottom two are notaccessible, a plastic 'wall' is blocking them. You have two options now. You can cut the plastic and bend it a bit, as seen in the next image, or you can dissassbmle the whole optical moduleof the projector and remove it from the plastic housing. I opted for the first option, but the second optionis valid and not that complicated. It's your call.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-149" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_20160121_183950-custom.jpg" alt="IMG_20160121_183950 (Custom).jpg" width="800" height="592" />

&nbsp;

Open all 4 bolts and remove the lens assembly. Replace the bolts with slightly longer M3 bolts, and put two M3 nuts between the lens and the projector. This will be our 'shim'. Should look something like this:

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-163" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_20160121_184629-custom.jpg" alt="IMG_20160121_184629 (Custom).jpg" width="800" height="676" />

You will need to remove the zoom ring (the gray plastic thing on top). It's connected with three small screws. Keep all parts you take off and the original four M3 bolts that held the lens. Put them in a box and markit. If you ever want to revert your projector back into normal operation mode, you will need those parts.

Connect the lens assembly back to the projector, it should look like this:

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-170" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_20160121_185551-custom.jpg" alt="IMG_20160121_185551 (Custom).jpg" width="800" height="649" />

You will still be able to change the zoom, using a small screwdriver inserted in that white plastic tube.

Put everything back together, and now you should be able to do this:

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-177" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_20160121_190117-custom.jpg" alt="IMG_20160121_190117 (Custom).jpg" width="800" height="642" />

It's really bright and can hurt your eyes, so please use a filter when focusing the projector. Also, once you have the zoom set, cover that top opening with opaque tape. You don't want UV light spilling out from there.

Next post will be about how to modify the LittleRP to mount the X152H. Don't worry though, It's a lot simpler than modding the projector.
<h3><strong>Taking it to the next level</strong></h3>
There is more you can do to this poor projector, if you are brave and technically inclined. The color wheel can be relocated and the bulb UV filter can be removed. Both will increase the UV output and reduce cure time. Some people go as far as replacing the bulb with a UV LED light, but I have not yet found any solid information about this. If you have done this, or know someone that did, please post some links in the comments.
<h3><strong>Nn&amp;Noot</strong></h3>
We use our LittleRP to printour unique sculpture jewelry, and then cast it in Sterling Silver. Check it out at <a href="http://NunAndNoot.Etsy.com">NunAndNoot.Etsy.com</a>

&nbsp;

&nbsp;]]></content:encoded>

    <wp:comment_content><![CDATA[Did you make a gasket to seal the gap created by the shim? Or did you leave it open like that?

For other types of short throw modifications, I have seen it mentioned that a gasket is necessary to keep dust from collecting on the formerly sealed light path for other projectors, but for the X152H, I don't know whether that path was sealed to begin with or whether leaving it open is fine.

Have you found any dust issues leaving it open, or did you build a gasket in a subsequent step?

I did not put a gasket there, but it sounds like a solid idea. I'm not sure if the optical engine is airtight to begin with. I will check later.]]></wp:comment_content>

<item>
  <title>What happens to PLA that has been sitting in water for 3.5 years? - An accidental experiment</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2016/02/29/what-happens-to-pla-that-has-been-sitting-in-water-for-3-5-years-an-accidental-experiment/</link>
  <pubDate>Mon, 29 Feb 2016 15:27:09 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://lazygeckoblog.wordpress.com/?p=193</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[When I just started out with 3D printing, somewhere back in 2012, I experimented with different ways to paint or stain my prints. I had some tiedyecolors lying around, so I decided to put a white PLA print in a jar of water and dye and see what happens. The jar was placed in the back of one of my workshop cupboards, and 8.2 seconds later I completely forgot about it.

Flash forward to spring 2016, I find the jar while cleaning out my workshop. Curious about this interesting and accidental experiment, I fished out the PLA part.

What do you think happens to PLA after being submerged in water for over 3.5 years?

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-207" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_20160229_101613.jpg" alt="IMG_20160229_101613.jpg" width="3200" height="2368" />

The answer: Absolutely nothing. It looks and feels exactly like the day it came off the printer.]]></content:encoded>

      <wp:meta_value>a:3:{s:4:"time";d:1480619727.778378009796142578125;s:5:"event";s:15:"status-approved";s:4:"user";s:11:"shacharweis";}</wp:meta_value>
    </wp:commentmeta>
  </wp:comment>
</item>
<item>
  <title>Building a laser engraver, Part 1</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2016/03/05/building-a-laser-engraver-part-1/</link>
  <pubDate>Sat, 05 Mar 2016 01:15:32 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://lazygeckoblog.wordpress.com/?p=214</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[My wife recently launched <a href="http://NunAndNoot.com">NunAndNoot.com</a>, where she sells her unique sculpture-like 3D printed jewelry. She also sells original artwork, and I wanted to laser engrave her drawings on wood. <a href="http://blog.huv.com/">Jon Hylands</a>loaned me his <a href="https://darklylabs.com/emblaser-overview/">Emblaser</a> unit to experiment. After playing around with it for two weeks this is what I learned:

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-260" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/banner2_small2.jpg" alt="banner2_small2" width="1000" height="278" />

<strong>1. Lasers are an awesome tool in a maker's workshop</strong>

The image above has laser engraved wood (the text of the right) and laser cut stencil (on the left, cut from poplar wood). Neat.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-314" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/a4_full.jpg" alt="a4_full" width="1920" height="1080" />

<strong>2. The Emblaserhas some glaring design flaws</strong>
<ul>
	<li>The CoreXY system is mounted on a single sheet of acrylic. You can see it visibly distort when the head changes direction. Acrylic is a<strong>terrible</strong>choice for a laser cutter (or a 3D printer) frame. It warps and distorts, and it will eventually crack.</li>
	<li>The laser head is mounted on two rods using two short linear bearings. Most systems of this design use 3 bearings, or two long bearings. With only two short bearings, the head has a lot of wobble to it. I tried to engrave a square and it came out with curved corners. That's just bad.</li>
	<li>The CoreXY belt rubs against one of the frame brackets. Just a matter of time until it wears out.</li>
</ul>
<strong>3. The software choices for a gcode based laser (which includes mostkits or DIY units)are slim</strong>

The Emblaser uses a standard Arudino running GRBL, and needs gcode to cut or engrave anything. It's really hard to find goodsoftware that can generate gcode for laser cutting or engraving. The commercial lasers all have proprietary software. There is a slew of InkScape plugins that can generate gcode from vectors but they are all buggy and unreliable. <a href="http://www.cambam.info/">CamBam </a>is the only thing I found so far that works.

There are several free and paid tools to make gcode fromimages, for engraving. They all suck. Even the paid ones are terrible. This drove me to write my own image engraving app, more on that later.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-274" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/img_20160227_110028.jpg" alt="IMG_20160227_110028" width="3200" height="2368" />
<h3><strong>Building my own</strong></h3>
I decided it's time I built my own laser engraver. I opted for a design that was as simple as possible, even if that meant using more parts or sacrificing speed. Lets go into more detail, but first, a warning:

<span style="color:#ff0000;"><strong>LASERS CAN PERMANENTLY DAMAGE YOUR SIGHT</strong></span>

Always -<strong>ALWAYS</strong> - wear proper eye protection when the laser is powered up. I can't stress this enough. Just glancing atthe laser dot as it hits an object can permanently damage your retina. You are going to need good eye protection that is rated for your laser wavelength, a lock on the door so that someone doesn't come in when the laser is on and a kill switch. I would also build a full enclosure for the laser unit.
<h4></h4>
<h4><img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-303" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/img_20160304_182032.jpg" alt="IMG_20160304_182032" width="1200" height="558" /></h4>
<h3><strong>OpenBuilds</strong></h3>
The frame and linear motion parts are all based on <a href="http://openbuildspartstore.com/">OpenBuilds </a>V-Slot extrusion and wheels system. V-Slot is neat and affordable, and I use it in many of my projects. Most of the parts for this laser engraver were salvaged from a giant delta printer that Iretired a few months ago.

The frame is held together with cheapHome Depotmetal "L" shape brackets and regular nuts &amp; bolts. I ran out of t-slot nuts.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-306" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/img_20160304_182043.jpg" alt="IMG_20160304_182043" width="1200" height="641" />
<h3><strong>Y</strong> <strong>Axis</strong></h3>
I opted for using two synchronized stepper motors for the y-axis. The motors move the central bar that is the X-axis. The downside is needing two motors (I had a few lying around), and that the x-axis motor is riding on the bar and adding mass and the need to handle wires. The upside is a that the whole system is very simple and straightforward.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-309" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/img_20160304_1820221.jpg" alt="IMG_20160304_182022" width="1200" height="665" />
<h3><strong>X Axis</strong></h3>
This is where the laser unit will be mounted (in part 2). Again, it's an OpenBuilds v-slot and wheel system.
<h3><strong>Video</strong></h3>
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=SL-pWUBceFk

&nbsp;

If anyone wants the STL files, write me a comment and I'll post them here. Honestly, they kinda suck. They have some silly design flaws that I was too lazy to fix and reprint and decided insteadto hack itwith a hot air gun, drilling and some metal wires.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>A review of the Wanhao Duplicator I3</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2016/03/08/a-review-of-the-wanhao-duplicator-i3/</link>
  <pubDate>Tue, 08 Mar 2016 17:26:44 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://lazygeckoblog.wordpress.com/?p=324</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[This review is actually 3 months old and was originally published on Reddit. I'm now posting it here, so I can further cash in on it.
<h6 style="text-align:center;"><img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-334" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/20140203_091120.jpg" alt="20140203_091120.jpg" width="1600" height="1200" />My giant delta, recently decommission due to lack of use and converted into a laser engraver</h6>
<h2><strong>Wanhao I3 Review</strong></h2>
About three years ago I got infected with 3DPrintinitus. I borrowed an Ultimaker, use it to design and print my own giant Delta printer. Since then I helped others build their own printers, and I take care of our MakerSpace printers (Printrbot play and Metal Plus).

Recently I starting running 3D printing workshops for teens, (check out <a href="http://www.ookpiklabs.com/">Ookpik Labs</a>!) so I needed a printer that I could lug around (we do the workshops in different locations). I order a Wanhao Duplicator I3 from their <a href="https://digitmakers.ca/shop/3d-printers/3d-printer-duplicator-i3-steel-frame-assembled/">Canadian distributor</a> and got it about 4 days later.

At $400 USD it's an incredibly capable and sturdy machine, all metal frame with a finish and machining that is surprisingly good.
<h6 style="text-align:center;"><img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-331" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/img_20151014_234112.jpg" alt="IMG_20151014_234112.jpg" width="3200" height="2368" />That is not poo</h6>
It has a big LCD, which is a nice touch, but I personally find 3D printer LCD's to be rather useless. You can navigate the options with the rotary button, but it's infuriatingly slow and cumbersome. I'll take a laptop or OctoPrint over that LCD any day.

The heated bed heats up relatively quick and uniform, the aluminum frame that holds it is too thin for my taste and a bit flimsy, but overall it's decent. It's highly recommended to add a glass printing surface on top of the aluminum heated bed.

<strong>Update</strong>: I have since upgraded the bed with a PEI sheet. I can't recommend this enough. It's the perfect bed material. Print will stick without warping and detach easily when the bed cools down. Zero maintenance.

The extruder and hotend are a bit underpowered, but again, surprisingly decent.
<h6 style="text-align:center;"><img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-343" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/img_20151019_110450.jpg" alt="IMG_20151019_110450.jpg" width="1000" height="464" />Firstprints, almost out of the box</h6>
Another thing I love about Wanhao is that they seem to be super attentive to community input, in fact many of the upgrades that I read about before getting the printer have already been includedin the version that I got. For example, the Y-axis pulley holder has been beefed up and no longer requires a zip-tie, the Z motors now have dampeners on them, and some other small details. For a printer that's been on the market for 6 months that is really impressive.

The major mod that I did make was this one: <a class="imgScanned" href="http://www.thingiverse.com/thing:921948">http://www.thingiverse.com/thing:921948</a>

I highly recommend it. It is very well designed and makes the frame <strong>a lot</strong> more rigid. Especially important if you intend on moving it around.

The major disadvantage for me right now is that the PSU/Controller box is connected by a thick bundle of wires to the printer and is not detachable. This makes it a bit tricky carry it around, since both the PSU and the printer are heavy. I am working on a way to mount and lock the PSU box onto the printer body for ease of transportation.

Another disadvantage is that it's not a fast printer. 50mm/s is really the top speed you can go before quality goes down, and the extruder starts missing steps. Some people report up to 80mm/s speeds can be achieved with a all-metal hotend. Honestly, I don't really care if a print takes 4 vs 6 hours. I'm still with the stock hotend.
<h3><strong>Conclusion</strong></h3>
If you want a really decent, full metal, <strong>cheap</strong> printer, that will give you great prints if you don't push it too fast, than get the Wanhao Duplicator I3. So far I love it and I feel that it's the best value currently on the market, and the only printer I can recommend in the sub-$500 category.

Here is one of my best printers, 11 hours in total, very hard on the retraction. The result is perfect:

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-352" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/img_3351.jpg" alt="IMG_3351.jpg" width="2871" height="3873" />

<a class="toggleImage expando-button collapsed collapsedExpando image commentImg">The model is my design and is available here:
</a><a class="imgScanned" href="https://www.youmagine.com/designs/the-shrine">https://www.youmagine.com/designs/the-shrine</a>]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>How to remove a DLP color wheel (and in my case also destroy the projector)</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2016/03/19/how-to-remove-a-dlp-color-wheel-and-in-my-case-also-destroy-the-projector/</link>
  <pubDate>Sat, 19 Mar 2016 21:17:55 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://lazygeckoblog.wordpress.com/?p=367</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Wanting faster curing time with my DLP printer (<a href="http://www.littlerp.com/">LittelRP</a>) I decided to further hack my projector and remove the DLP color wheel.<a href="http://tjsnyman.com/tjsnymanblogspot/?attachment_id=690">Here</a>is a nice explanation of what a color wheel doesand why it's there. For DLP 3D printing, the color wheel gets in the way and (supposedly) blocks some of our coveted UV light.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-377" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/img_20160224_185342.jpg" alt="IMG_20160224_185342.jpg" width="3092" height="2368" />

Here is my poor Acer X152H, without the top cover. That red circle is the DLP wheel. We will need to disassemble quite a bit of stuff before we can reach it. Take out the light bulb, then open all those screws on the electronics board on the top right. Unscrew the lens optics housing and pry it all loose.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-385" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/img_20160224_1906451.jpg" alt="IMG_20160224_190645.jpg" width="3200" height="2368" />

There is the DLP wheel, but sadly we can't just disconnect it. The projector synchronizesthe frame-rate with the rotation of the wheel, without the wheel it won't even turn on. Our only option is to relocate it.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-387" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/img_20160224_190756.jpg" alt="IMG_20160224_190756.jpg" width="2680" height="1308" />

Since the insides of the projector arepacked tight, I opted to mount it outside, glued to the top of the unit. I even covered it with a small plastic housing, so nothing would touch it. Remember - it needs to be somewhere it can spin freely, or the projector won't function properly. Another option is to keep the wheel motor and remove the glass. I am unsure ifthe projector can cope with this, as the wheel will have a different mass and rotation speed.

Another thing I did at this point was to remove the bulb UV filter, which is a small glass window that sits right on the bulb itself.

<hr />

<h3><span style="color:#ff6600;">EDIT: DO NOT REMOVE THE UV FILTER
</span></h3>
A commenter has mentioned thatafter removing the UV filter the same lens exploded inside his projector.<strong>The UV filter acts as a heat shield, and removing it will kill your projector.</strong>

<hr />

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-401" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/img_20160224_191554.jpg" alt="IMG_20160224_191554.jpg" width="2368" height="1496" />
<h2><strong>And now for the bad news</strong></h2>
While putting everything back together, I accidentally placed the main cooling fan backwards. It was sucking air instead of blowing it on the light bulb. A few minutes into the first test print the projector overheated and shut down. Further inspection lead to a sad discovery, the first glass lens in the light path was shattered and half melted it's way out of the plastic housing.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-403" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/img_20160318_220124.jpg" alt="IMG_20160318_220124" width="3200" height="1860" /><img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-404" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/03/img_20160318_220114.jpg" alt="IMG_20160318_220114" width="2412" height="1676" />

The projector still works, but the light output is now a fraction of what it should be, rendering it almostuseless. Finding a replacement lensis impossible, I have no idea what arethe required optical properties or even the overallshape. It exploded beyond reconstruction.
<h2><strong>Conclusions</strong></h2>
Honestly, I have no idea. Did the lens explode because I removed the wheel or because I installed the cooling fan wrong? Maybe it was a combination of the two? Perhaps the DLP wheel also acts as a heat shield. I really don't know.

What do you think? If you have any ideas or questions, drop me a comment below.

&nbsp;]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>Building a laser engraver, Part 2</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2016/03/29/building-a-laser-engraver-part-2/</link>
  <pubDate>Tue, 29 Mar 2016 22:17:55 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://lazygeckoblog.wordpress.com/?p=428</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[While waiting for my L-Cheap 3.5W laser to arrive, I decide to fit my layer engraver with a drawing head so I can test the software toolchain. I hooked up two 12v solenoids to the hotend port of the Azteeh X5 controller board. The Smoothieboard firmware has an option to define "<a href="http://smoothieware.org/switch">Switches</a>", these are veryhandy andallows you to make pretty much anything base on the Smoothieboard hardware. Kudos for that.

Solenoids make really poor linear motion systems, and also they get very hot very quickly (hot enough to deform PLA).I never intended on building a drawbot, this was a fun weekend project. I might design a better drawhead in the future, based on a servo rather thansolenoids.

Anyway, here is the first and last "print" of the LazyGecko drawbot, sped up x4.

https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=UYDq1glPt6E]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>Resin Casting - Tips and Tricks</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2016/04/03/resin-casting-tips-and-tricks/</link>
  <pubDate>Sun, 03 Apr 2016 15:33:53 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://lazygeckoblog.wordpress.com/?p=442</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Got a physical object you want to duplicate? Want to make 100 copies of a 3D print, but can't afford to wait 100 days? Do you want a robust sculpture but don't have a kiln? Resin casting to the rescue!
<h2><strong>Getting started</strong></h2>
You will need an item to be duplicated, 2-part silicone for the mold (or mould, depends where you live), mold release spray, 2-part resin and some optional additives. I recommend <a href="http://www.smooth-on.com/Silicone-Rubber-an/c2_1113_1136/index.html">Oomoo</a>product line, they are easy to use and affordable. My wife (<a href="http://NunAndNoot.com">NunAndNoot.com</a>) carved a few really nice sculptures out of soap stone.

[gallery ids="462,460" type="rectangular"]

&nbsp;

I wont go into lots of detail about the process itself, because it's already explained in a hundred youtube videos. Here is a good one:

https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=TgLKwMV7NbM

&nbsp;

I do have a few tip, tricks and warning though. Here they are:
<h2><strong>TIP: Lego Mold</strong></h2>
The silicone is expensive, if your object is irregular it can be trick to make a mold that conserves material. The solution is Lego. This pic pretty much explains the whole process:

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-468" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/04/o7w73hd.jpg" alt="o7w73hd" width="4224" height="2543" />
<h2><strong>TIP: Sand additive</strong></h2>
In addition to the usual additive powders like iron or bronze (expensive), I tried beach sand. The result is interesting, and differs greatly with the type of resin used (clear or white). The sand sinks to the bottom, so don't expect a uniform distribution.
<h2><strong>Warning:Shelf life</strong></h2>
Smooth-on will gloss this over, but the truth is that these products have a very short shelf life. Resin will start going bad after about 6 months, even if it's in a sealed container. Don't buy big volumes unless you intend on using it. Bad resin will foamwhen curing, and will overflow from the mold and is generally nasty.

&nbsp;]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>Resume</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/cv/</link>
  <pubDate>Fri, 29 Apr 2016 15:30:06 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://lazygeckoblog.wordpress.com/cv/</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<table style="border:0 solid #ffffff;" cellspacing="0" cellpadding="10">
<tbody>
<tr>
<td style="border:0 solid #ffffff;" valign="top" width="80%">
<h2>Introduction</h2>
</td>
<td style="border:0 solid #ffffff;" valign="top">
<h4><strong><a href="https://lazygeckoblog.wordpress.com/contact/">Contact Me</a></strong></h4>
</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

<hr />

I have over 20 years of broad R&amp;D experience - from managing the R&amp;D in a startup environment to developing enterprise solutions in HP labs and leading R&amp;D teams in the Israeli Navy Computer Center. I have worked in many areas, including Computer Vision, databases, web development, 2D/3D graphics and image/video processing. I've worked with big clients such as Disney, Sears &amp; L'Oral. I build 3D printers and quadcotpers, I teach 3D modeling and 3D printer (among other subjects) and I've done hardware-software integration and robotics. I bring with me acute analytical skills, system-wide vision, experience with clients and know-how in R&amp;D work methodologies.
<h2><strong>2016  current:Packet39</strong></h2>

<hr />

Founder and CTO of Packet39 -ateam of professional software developers and academics, working together to provide software &amp; hardware solutions. Packet39thrives in the space between code and the physical world and develops solutions that sense, manipulate and move around in the environment.

&nbsp;

<header>
<h2><strong>2014  2016 : Senior Developer at SkySquirrel Technologies</strong></h2>

<hr />

SkySquirrel Technologies Inc. is a crop-analytics company that develops drone-based technology for monitoring crop health, with a primary focus on improving crop yields and reducing costs at commercial vineyards.
<h3><strong>Accomplishments:</strong></h3>
<ul>
	<li>Developed a stand-alone camera system that includes a board level computer and multiple sensors at different wavelengths. The unit has a light sensor and uses IMU data, GPS coordinates and the position of the sun to calculated reflected light. The system is autonomous and can be mounted on any drone or aircraft.</li>
	<li>Developed backend pipeline for image processing, aligning and stitching.</li>
	<li>I created the GUI and communication infrastructure to control the camera over radio and get live feedback while the aircraft is in the air.</li>
	<li>Worked with open-source project to adapt to SSTs requirements</li>
</ul>
<h3><strong>Technologies:</strong></h3>
<ul>
	<li>Python, CFFI</li>
	<li>C++, OpenCV</li>
	<li>C#, EMGU and MySQL</li>
	<li>Django web stack</li>
</ul>
<h2>2009  2014 : CTO at Imagin8</h2>

<hr />

</header>Imagin8 is a Toronto based startup company that develops technologies for interactive projections and displays using cameras and image processing. People can interact with Imagin8's products with touch, movement or gestures. Imagin8's products are mainly used for out-of-home advertising, information kiosks and promotional events. I designed and implemented most of Imagin8's products and software infrastructure. I recruited developers and managed the R&amp;D team. I had a key part in creating a self-sustaining startup company, with large scale international clients.

<header>
<h3>Accomplishments</h3>
</header>
<ul>
	<li>Worked with tier one clients (such as Disney, Sears, ) and created interactive content that took part in global</li>
	<li>marketing campaigns and was installed in major airports and malls worldwide.</li>
	<li>Developed algorithms for detecting touch and finger tips using cameras and a specially designed display.</li>
	<li>Designed and implemented a multi-touch application engine running under XNA for 2D and 3D user interface.</li>
	<li>Developed algorithms for detecting people and gestures using 3D time-of-flight cameras.</li>
	<li>Built a web-based management product that remotely controls and monitors many distributed systems.</li>
	<li>Managed and directed a team of developers, graphic designers and technicians.</li>
</ul>
<h3>Technologies</h3>
<ul>
	<li>Image processing using C++, OpenCV and EMGU</li>
	<li>3D and 2D applications using XNA and WPF</li>
	<li>C# and .Net 4.0 with Multitouch framework</li>
	<li>Native Mobile development (iPhone and Android)</li>
	<li>Cross-platform mobile development (PhoneGAP, Mono, MonoGames)</li>
	<li>Web platforms (Angular.JS, Node.JS, ASP.net)</li>
</ul>
<header>
<h2>2005  2009 : R&amp;D manager at EyeClick</h2>

<hr />

</header>EyeClick is a startup company that specializes in interactive games, using projectors and body movements. I was the first developer in EyeClick and I recruited and managed the R&amp;D team. I developed core components of EyeClicks software infrastructure. EyeClick now has over 20 employees and worldwide sales.

<header>
<h3>Accomplishments</h3>
</header>
<ul>
	<li>Created a self-funding startup company with 3M$ annual revenue and over 20 employees.</li>
	<li>Developed algorithms for detecting movement and gestures in camera video streams, one of which was patented and accepted.</li>
	<li>Designed and implemented an extensive 2D engine under OpenGL that allows easy and fast development of complex interactive graphical applications and games.</li>
	<li>Created a very successful 8-meter-wide multi-projection interactive wall that was exhibited in the GE pavilion at the 2008 Beijing Olympics. This was the largest pavilion in the Olympic village and attracted over 70k visitors.</li>
	<li>Managed a team of 4 engineers.</li>
</ul>
<h3>Technologies</h3>
<ul>
	<li>Image processing using C++ and OpenCV</li>
	<li>3D and 2D applications using C++ and OpenGL</li>
	<li>C# and ASP.Net</li>
	<li>2D using Silverlight 2.0 and Flash AS3</li>
</ul>
<header>
<h2>2004  2005 : Weizmann Institute of Science</h2>

<hr />

</header>With guidance and supervision of Prof. Ronen Basri I developed an algorithm for extrapolating 3D information from a 2D image.
<h3>Technologies</h3>
<ul>
	<li>Matlab, Multigrid, ANN</li>
</ul>
<header>
<h2>2001  2004 : Senior software developer at Mercury interactive</h2>

<hr />

</header>Mercury interactive (acquired by HP) develops tools to software testing, project management and application monitoring for large organizations. I was part of the development team for Topaz, a product that allows real-time load measurement and monitoring of large scale web applications.

<header>
<h3>Accomplishments</h3>
</header>
<ul>
	<li>Developed complex server-side modules enabling the user to create their own reporting system.</li>
	<li>Worked with clients to understand needs and improve the product.</li>
	<li>Created an extensive Database abstraction layer to achieve DB vendor independence.</li>
	<li>Designed and developed the SLA module of the Topaz product, including web reports and server-side logic.</li>
</ul>
<h3>Technologies</h3>
<ul>
	<li>Java, JSP, Oracle and SQL Server</li>
</ul>
<header>
<h2>2000  2001 : R&amp;D Manager at iBetcha.com</h2>

<hr />

</header>iBetcha was a startup company that developed and operated a gaming website. Players could bet on the outcome of world events or created their own bets. Betting was done with points that could be traded for prizes.

<header>
<h3>Accomplishments</h3>
</header>
<ul>
	<li>Managed two development teams, total of 8 developers.</li>
	<li>Designed and created a complex, dynamic and fast-changing website, with over 50k unique users per day.</li>
</ul>
<header>
<h2>1995  2000 : Software developer and team leader at Israeli Navy Computer Center</h2>

<hr />

</header>During my military service I took part in developing several database driven systems that serviced the Israeli Navy.

<header>
<h3>Accomplishments</h3>
</header>
<ul>
	<li>Managed a team of 2 developers.</li>
	<li>Developed high-security and highly reliable software for use in war time or naval field exercises.</li>
	<li>Developed communication and data-mining applications that were used by high ranking personnel across the Navy.</li>
</ul>
<header>
<h2>Education</h2>

<hr />

</header>2004  2005 : Courses in Image processing and Computer Vision, Weizmann Institute of Science, Israel.
1998  2004 : B.Sc. in Computer Science at the Open University, Israel.]]></content:encoded>

<td style="border:0 solid #ffffff;" valign="top">I made a VR game! It's called <a href="http://store.steampowered.com/app/587730">Orbital Injection</a>, the goal is to toss as many objects as you can into a stable orbit without them crashing into the sun. It's built for the HTC Vive and is a room-scale experience and is now available on <a href="http://store.steampowered.com/app/587730">Steam</a>.</td>

If you want to try it yourself, download the app <a href="https://shacharweis.github.io/static/Packet39.apk">here</a> (Android only). You'll need a Packet39 <a href="https://shacharweis.github.io/images/BizCard.png">card</a>, print one if you don't have any.</td>

<td style="border:0 solid #ffffff;" valign="top">I founded <a href="http://www.ookpiklabs.com/">OokpikLabs.com</a> in spring 2015, where I teach technology themed workshops to adults and teens. <td style="border:0 solid #ffffff;" valign="top">People walking on interactive water display, GE pavilion at the Beijing 2008 Olympics. Machine visioncameras detect motion and track players, send the silhouette data to an XNA-basedapplication layer which renders a water effect using pixel &amp; vertex shaders.</td>
</tr>

I developed both the tracking software and the displayengine. The game is fully customizable, the client can add banners and personalize all the graphics.</td>
<td style="border:0 solid #ffffff;" valign="top"><img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-529" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/04/soccer.jpg" alt="soccer" width="640" height="446" /></td>

<item>
  <title>Free &amp; paid 3D modeling tools, for 3D printing</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2016/05/07/free-paid-3d-modeling-tools-for-3d-printing/</link>
  <pubDate>Sat, 07 May 2016 14:38:02 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=574</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<h3>Which CAD should I use?</h3>
I've been asked this question so many times I decided to make a blog post about it.
<h2>Free CAD tools</h2>
<a href="http://TinkerCAD.com">TinkerCAD.com</a>- If you absolutely have no experience with 3D modeling or design, this should be your first stop. They have a slew of fully interactive tutorials that guide you every step of the way. TinkerCAD is the only child-friendly CAD tool that I know of. If you are serious about CAD, you will outgrow it very quickly, but it will give you an excellent basic understanding that you can then carry over to other CAD tools on this list. The downside (beyond the very low glass ceiling) is that due to it's popularity in the education system, the website often slows down to a crawl during US school hours. TinkerCAD is free for hobby use.

<a href="http://OnShape.com">OnShape.com</a> -This is like TinkerCAD for grown ups. Developed by a bunch of guys that left SolidWorks, this is a cloud-based in-browser CAD tool. It's parametric (meaning that at any point you can go back and change most aspects of your design) and it's very powerful. It's rather new and still missing some important features, but it's well worth getting into. They have excellent video tutorials. The free plan limits the number of projects you can save on their servers, but is otherwise fully functional. It's even free for commercial use, which is a rare feature on this list.

<a href="http://www.autodesk.com/products/fusion-360/overview">Fusion 360 </a>-Autodesk seem to love making (or buying) products that have wide overlaps and sometimes it's very confusing. Fusion360 is one of their CAD tools, and it's a very nice one. It's a mix of cloud and local, where the application itself is on your computer but you must login to the cloud to use it. I personally find this very annoying. Fusion360 is free for hobby use and commercial use (up to $100k yearly earning!)andis gaining a lot of popularity in the community due to this.

<a href="http://www.openscad.org/">OpenSCAD </a>- OpenSCAD stands out because it's not exactly what you'd expect from a CAD tool. In OpenSCAD you write code to create 3Dobjects. This has several pros and cons. Pro: It's very easy to go back and change stuff. For example, lets say you made a whole range or parts and then found that you ran out of M5 bolts. Going back and changing all the holes to M4 is trivial, just change one value in the code and regenerate. Con: It takes forever to make anything. I'm a software developer by profession, and even for me it takes about 10x longer to make an objects in OpenSCAD compared to a "normal" CAD tool.OpenSCAD is OpenSource and free.

<a href="http://www.sketchup.com/">Sketchup </a>-Let's get this out of the way. Sketchup is used extensively in the 3D printing community, but it sucks. It really does. It was designed for architecture, for making 3D models that render nice and look good on screen. It's was not designed for making solid objects or mechanical parts, and it's really bad at it. It will happily generate broken STLs, with missing faces (holes), self intersections, etc. You will find that pretty much any complex object made in Sketchup needs repair before it can be printed. This is a pain, and with so many excellent &amp; free tools out there, there isn't any reason to use Sketchup. Sketchup free, but only for hobby use.

<a href="http://www.123dapp.com/design">123D Design</a> -Another of Autodesk's CAD tools. I'm not a fan of 123D Design, it's rather limited and tends to crash often. I don't recommend it.

<a href="http://www.freecadweb.org/">FreeCAD </a>-The only OpenSource CAD that I know of. It's been around for a long time, it's parametric and has a lot of features. Sadly, it's absolutely terrible. The GUI is borderline unusable and it crashes a lot. I keep coming back to FreeCAD every year, and every year I'm brokenhearted. Avoid it for now.

<a href="https://www.blender.org/">Blender</a>- Blender is.. crazy. It does everything. From 3D animations, characters, CAD, sculpting, you name it, it in there somewhere. OpenSource and free, it's insanely powerful but also has a very steep learning curve. A lot of functions are only accessiblevia keyboard commands, so if you do dive into Blender make sure you start memorizing those right out of the gate.

&nbsp;
<h2>Paid CAD tools</h2>
<a href="http://www.solidworks.com/">SolidWorks</a>- The king of CAD, SolidWorks is a top of the line and feature packed parametric CAD tool, with a high price tag. If you are professional industrial designer (or studying to be one), you are probably using SolidWorks.

<a href="https://www.rhino3d.com/">Rhino3D </a>- My personal favorite of this list, Rhino3D has been around for a very long time. It's mature and powerful, has an incredibly intuitive command line interface and in my honest opinion, it is the easiest to pickup for newcomers. With the amazing GrassHopper plugin it can become parametric and math driven. Well worth checking out, Rhino3D has a fully-functional 90 day trial period and large discounts for students.

&nbsp;
<h2>Free digital sculpting tools</h2>
<a href="http://pixologic.com/sculptris/">Sculptris </a>- This started as a hobby project and was later bought by PixelLogic (who make ZBrush), probably so they can stop development and make sure it doesn't compete with Zbrush. Unlike a CAD tool, sculpting software allows you to model organic and "messy" objects, using your mouse as if you are sculpting clay. Sculptris doesn't have a lot of features, but you can actually get a lot done with it. This <a href="https://www.etsy.com/ca/listing/265899732">ring</a>for example, was made entirely in Sculptris. It's fun way to get into digital sculpting, and it's free but it's also limited and tends to crash a lot. A LOT.
<h2>Paiddigital sculpting tools</h2>
<a href="https://pixologic.com/">ZBrush</a>- The king of digital sculpting. To say that ZBrush has a lot of feature is the understatement of the year. It has features inside features. It also has the worst GUI that I've ever seen on a commercial application. An absolute nightmare of menus inside menus inside options inside panels. PixelLogic opted to create their own GUI and don't use any of the operating system's GUI elements. This means it doesn't scale with your screen DPI settings. ZBrush is unusable on high resolution screens because the fonts are buttons are *tiny*. Boooo.

<a href="http://www.autodesk.com/products/mudbox/overview">Mudbox </a>- Another Autodesk app on this list, mudbox is rather new and seems to have a lot of features. I have no tried it so I can't comment much about it. It has a monthly subscription payment model.

&nbsp;

&nbsp;]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>WaxCast by MakerJuice, first results</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2016/05/09/waxcast-by-makerjuice-first-results/</link>
  <pubDate>Mon, 09 May 2016 04:17:51 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=651</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[I've recently started a small jewelry business with my wife (<a href="https://www.etsy.com/shop/NunAndNoot">NunAndNoot</a>), she is the designer and I'm the technician. Our biggest struggle is to reduce the cost of making our items, and to do so I need to be able to 3D print the items at home and cast directly from the prints.

Enter, <a href="https://www.makerjuice.com/products/waxcast">WaxCast</a> by <a href="https://www.makerjuice.com/">MakerJuice</a>. I've tried several castable resins (including B9 and FunToDo), and so far WaxCast has been the best to print with. Detail is great, and support break off easily and cleanly. I still don't know how well it actually casts, that will be tested next week.

I'm currently printing at 4s exposure time at 50um layers, 6mm Z travel and slicing using the terrible Creation Workshop (still can't find any alternative that actually works with support structure).

<img class="alignnone  wp-image-652" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/05/img_20160509_000035.jpg" alt="IMG_20160509_000035.jpg" width="632" height="675" />

Here is the latest prints, notice they have a few random bad layers. I'm still trying to figure out where thosecomes from. Any ideas?

&nbsp;]]></content:encoded>

  <title>How many desktop 3D printers are in the wild?</title>

  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Someone asked me this, and I decided to look into it. Lets try to dig up some numbers.

&nbsp;

<a href="http://www.makerbot.com/">MakerBot</a>- I could write a whole post about MakerBot, why they are terrible, why you should never buy one. <item>
  <title>Building a laser engraver, Part 3</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2016/05/19/building-a-laser-engraver-part-3/</link>
  <pubDate>Thu, 19 May 2016 13:31:07 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=689</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[After a run of bad luck with <a href="http://robots-everywhere.com/re_site/purchase/l-cheapo/">L-Cheapo</a> units (kudos totheir excellent and responsive support) I decided to get a much more expensive <a href="https://jtechphotonics.com/?product=new-2-8w-laser-and-2-5amp-safety-compliant-driver-kit-with-us-style-power-adapter">JTechPhotonics</a>unit. I bought the 2.8W laser, due to the smaller dot size (0.18mm) compared to the 3.5W, and the price difference.

The JTech unit is superbly built, their documentation is excellent and you can tell a lot of thought and planning went into the board design. At $335 I expected nothing less. There are very few players in this market, if you want a cheap laser you can buy the components off ebay and build it yourself. Be warned though, blue diode are super sensitive to static discharge and it's really easy to destroy a diode just by holding it wrong.

I mounted the laser board next to the Azteeg X5 that is driving the engraver, hooked up the power, connected the TTL line to the Azteeg fan PWM output, and it works.

Here is one of the first cuts I made:

https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ntSUwn7i5Bw&amp;feature=em-upload_owner

&nbsp;

I still have a lot of issues to solve, I need to wire thecooling on the laser diode, which will considerably extend it's lifespan and also allow me to drive it with a bit more current.

The software toolchain is also a major headache, the vast majority of free software for converting vector to gcode are inkscape plugins, none of which seem to work reliably (or at all). I'm currently experimenting with <a href="http://www.cambam.info/">CamBam</a>, but this also only<em>kinda</em> works, since it was not designed for laser cutters. You have to use post-processing on the gcode to make it suitable for a laser cutter. More on the software in an upcoming blog post (Part 4).

<img class="alignnone  wp-image-710" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/05/20160518_232342.jpg" alt="20160518_232342" width="453" height="380" />]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>An overview of the 3D printer Kickstaters - Part 1 - The Disasters</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2016/05/20/an-overview-of-the-3d-printer-kickstaters-part-1-the-disasters/</link>
  <pubDate>Fri, 20 May 2016 19:04:15 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=716</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[&nbsp;

A list of 3D printer kickstaters that were successfully funded and what happened to them afterwards.
<h2>Complete Disaster</h2>
<h3><a href="https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/1650950769/rigidbot-3d-printer/updates">RigidBot</a>2013, $335</h3>
RigidBot raised over $1,000,000 on Kickstater back in 2013. The design seemed solid but that wasn't enough. Some backers got their printers butreported poor performance and a slew of technical problems.On September 2015 the company folded, stopped shipping printers, stopped issuing refunds and ignored all communication since then. The Kickstater comments page still has people clamoring for a refund, which will probably never come.
<h3><a class="imgScanned" href="https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/117421627/the-peachy-printer-the-first-100-3d-printer-and-sc/description">Peachy Printer </a> 2013 , $100</h3>
One of the most spectacular disasters on this list, Peachy Printer raised a lot of money (over 1m from kickstater, indigogo and government funding) back in 2013. They then went on spending most of the money on salaries and a house. Yup, one of the founders allegedly stole over $300k and built a house. They announced thetheftwith the most bizarre video and cringe-worthy video I've ever seen, including melodramatic background music and super sad faces in closeup. A few days ago they released a video of a flimsy ad-hocprinter in action, showing almost noprogress compared to their KS prototype, after 18 months and $1,000,000 spent.
<h3><a class="imgScanned" href="https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/pirate3d/the-buccaneer-the-3d-printer-that-everyone-can-use">Pirate3d, Buccaneer </a>2013 , $247</h3>
Raised almost $1.5m, Pirate3D delayed shipping again and again, and eventually announced that not all the backers will get their printer. Amazingly, they are still selling printers on their website.
<h3><a class="imgScanned" href="https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/535786699/flxarm-low-cost-precision-robotic-arm/description">FLX.ARM</a>2014 ,$1800</h3>
Featuring a unique SCARA-based 3D printer, they raised almost $90k on KS. Over a year later, there are still updates but the printer itself is nowhere close to shipping. Also, there is a reason why nobody makes SCARA 3D printers, it's just a terrible design choice forthis application.
<h3><a class="imgScanned" href="https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/ez3d/phoenix-3d-printer/description">Phoenix3d, EZ3D</a>2013 , $349</h3>
Raised almost $110k in 2013, went bankrupt in 2015. Very few backers got their printer.
<h3><a class="imgScanned" href="https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/826799607/ibox-nano-worlds-smallest-least-expensive-3d-print/description">iBox Nano</a>2014 , $189</h3>
Raised over $450k in 2014, they actually shipped "printers" but they didn't really work. Terrible software, poor hardware choices, super slow. They tried a second kickstater that failed to fund.
<h3><a class="imgScanned" href="https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/fsl/pegasus-touch-laser-sla-3d-printer-low-cost-high-q/description">Pegasus Touch</a>2014 ,$1750</h3>
A kickstater From Full Spectrum Laser (a company that I have zero love for and that is very hostile towards makers/tinkers and loves DRM) ran this KS and raked in $819k. Many backers did not get their printers, those who did are reporting major issues. The company has since stopped all communications via their KS page and are heavily moderating their forums. Just like their laser-cutters, this printer must be online-activated and if you buy it used you need to pay a transfer fee just to use your device. No thanks.
<h3><a class="imgScanned" href="https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/sculptify/david-the-next-step-in-3d-printing/description">David, Sculptify</a>2014 , $2745</h3>
A printer that was supposed to run on pellents instead of filament, they raised $110k back in 2014. Very few backers (if any) actually got their printer and the company has since quietly vanished.
<h3><a class="imgScanned" href="https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/helix3d/helix-a-new-twist-on-3d-printing/description">Helix3d</a>2013 ,$4750</h3>
One of the highest priced printers on this list, Helix3D tried (and failed) to make what was basically a scaled up but otherwise very standard reprap. over 100 backed, but not all printers shipped. I am unsure if refunds were issued, the company has since shut down.
<h3><a href="https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/cobblebot/cobblebot-3d-printer">CobbleBot</a>2014 , $299</h3>
I'm afraid to write anything about CobbleBot.
I'll just drop these links here:

Over 10,000 <a href="https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/cobblebot/cobblebot-3d-printer/comments">comments </a>on the KS page
<a href="http://cobbleflop.cohesion3d.com/t/new-cobblebot-legal-claims/129">http://cobbleflop.cohesion3d.com/t/new-cobblebot-legal-claims/129</a>
<a href="https://3dprint.com/tag/cobblebot-scam/">https://3dprint.com/tag/cobblebot-scam/
</a><a href="https://3dprint.com/58371/cobblebot-shipment/">COBBLEBOT HOLDING BACK SHIPMENTS TO CUSTOMERS FOR DEFAMATION</a>

&nbsp;

&nbsp;

&nbsp;

&nbsp;]]></content:encoded>

  <title>Building a laser engraver, Part 4</title>
