<item>
  <title>A review of Avatar VR gloves by Neurodigital</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/?p=1491</link>
  <pubDate>Mon, 30 Nov -0001 00:00:00 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=1491</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[&nbsp;

My company (<a href="http://packet39.com/">Packet39</a>) develops VR and AR safety and <a href="https://callmevice.com/2017/09/19/vr-for-industry/">training</a> applications for the manufacturing and power industry. We could use hand tracking in many of our projects, and while the <a href="https://www.leapmotion.com/">LeapMotion </a>works well when your hands are "in the air", it fails miserably when you are holding or touching objects. We decided to buy and test every VR glove on the market. Here is a review of<a href="https://www.neurodigital.es/avatarvr/">Avatar VR</a> by NeuroDigital.es. A set of two gloves set us back 2000 Euro before shipping and taxes. Not a small amount.
<h3>Overview</h3>
<img class="  wp-image-1507 aligncenter" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/11/img_20171109_215503.jpg" alt="IMG_20171109_215503.jpg" width="317" height="178" />

Included in the box were two gloves, two wrist sensors, two arm sensors and one chest band sensor. Also included are two long mini-USB cables. Interestingly, component #9 in the diagram below (AVRLink) was not included. I don't know what it does or if it's required.

<img class=" aligncenter" src="https://www.neurodigital.es/wp-content/uploads/2016/12/1.jpg" width="528" height="296" />

The gloves have IMUs that can do positional tracking, but it's relative and not absolute. That means it needs a point of reference, and that point of reference is the chest sensor. The system will not work without it.

<img class=" aligncenter" src="https://www.neurodigital.es/wp-content/uploads/2016/12/2.jpg" width="371" height="311" />
<h3>Operation</h3>
Each glove has two RGB lights to indicate status and they often blink a confusing set of colors and patterns. Most are not documented anywhere, so I'm not sure what they mean. They also have a micro-usb port for wired connection and a bluetooth module for wireless.

&nbsp;

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-1534" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/11/img_20171109_215943-1-e1510498171416.jpg" alt="img_20171109_215943-1.jpg" width="5312" height="2979" />

As you can see in the picture above, the glove connects to the rest of the sensors with a flat plastic connector, which doesn't lock and is easily detached. I think this is to make the process of putting on the gloves easier. However, when you actually have the gloves on your hands, connecting that plug is almost impossible. More on that in a bit.

<img class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-1585" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/11/3.png?w=854" alt="3.PNG" width="427" height="286" />

According to the documentation, the gloves will sometimes require calibration upon power-up. This consists of putting all the sensors on a flat surface, and letting it blink the LEDs for about 30 seconds. The docs are not clear if this needs to be done every time, or only the when the lights are blinking. So far, the lights have blinked every time I turned on the gloves, so I calibrated it every time. Again, I really don't know if this is required or not.

At first my computer didn't see the gloves' Bluetooth, but after I replaces my Bluetooth dongle with a newer one it worked without a problem. The gloves connect to the Neurodigital software, which is called<a href="https://www.neurodigital.es/developer/downloads/">ND Suite</a>. Currently only Windows is supported. ND Suite offers some basic feedback on the glove's sensors, and allows you to test the actuators. There are 10actuators in each glove, they are basically little vibrating buzzers.

https://youtu.be/BxtqVgfD0fA

&nbsp;

As you can see in the video above, the glove detects gestures with four conductive pads, three on the fingers and one on the palm. I found that sometimes the contact is not registered if you don't press hard enough. The pads can slide and move around, and that can also cause a failure to register.
<h3>Comfort</h3>
<img class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-1568" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/11/img_20171110_1513012.jpg?w=704" alt="IMG_20171110_151301.jpg" width="352" height="374" />

These gloves are <strong>clunky</strong>. There is just no way to sugar coat it, getting into the gloves is a huge hassle. You need to put on all the straps first, while the gloves dangle from the end of the wires, and take great care that the connector won't detach. The bands are elastic but not Velcro, they use an annoying double ring setup. I guess it makes it easier to tighten, but it also leaves a lot of dangling loose ends.

It's crucial that the sensors don't move around, so the bands need to be tight. They itch. They limit blood circulation. It's not fun.The chest band is particularly annoying, it kept sliding down no matter how much I tightened it. The chest sensor is not attached to the band and will slide sideways. I don't know if this is a design flaw, if it's deliberate or if it's just of no consequences.

The gloves themselves are bulky and have a lot of <em>stuff</em> inside then. Due to the actuators on the fingertips, doing anything while wearing the glove is almost impossible. I tried typing on my keyboard and couldn't. It also gets hot.
<h3>Testing the gloves in VR</h3>
There are only two VR demos that are available to download (as of the writing of this review). ZeroGravity and Carnival.

[caption id="attachment_1600" align="aligncenter" width="468"]<img class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-1600" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/11/4.png?w=936" alt="4.PNG" width="468" height="234" /> Piano and Bubbles both lead to page that says "coming soon"[/caption]

Both require you to stand in a certain pose before you can start, to calibrate the relative position of the gloves. Here is the carnival demo:

https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=RNn6lJQKD48&amp;t=6s

&nbsp;

I spent a few hours tying to get the best performance out of the system. I did calibration every time I powered up the gloves, I read the documentation twice, I moved around the sensor bands. I redid the pose calibration. I rebooted. Nothing made much of a difference.

There are two very big problems here:
<ol>
	<li>The positional tracking is off. Way off. It's doesn't match the real hand position <strong>at all</strong>. The virtual hands will rotate, shift and fly off in random directions. When walking around the VR play space, the hands don't really follow you. They move somewhere, just not where you are going. In the video above, it was extremely difficult to get the VR hands into camera view, as they were floating somewhere behind me.</li>
	<li>The datastream from the gloves seems to sputter and hang all the time. You can see it in the ND Suite (the screen with all the little XYZ arrows). You can clearly see it in the video above. This is a wireless/Bluetooth problem (it doesn't happen when using the USB connection). I don't have any other Bluetooth devices connected to my computer.</li>
</ol>
<h3>Conclusion</h3>
As it stands right now, I can't recommend this product to anyone, for the following reasons:
<ol>
	<li>Positional tracking is completely broken. Maybe I'm doing something wrong, but for life of me I can't figure out what, and I'm a very technical person, a VR developer and I've been in this space for two years.</li>
	<li>Sputter and lag when wireless (can't help but notice that in all their videos use the wired connection for the gloves)</li>
	<li>Lack of documentation and troubleshooting guides. I've read every word in the documentation twice and I still don't know what the LED colors mean.</li>
	<li>Clunky, poor design of straps and connectors.</li>
</ol>
I've contacted Neurodigital about all these problems and waited a week before posting this review. I did not hear back from them. If they do contact me, I will update here.]]></content:encoded>

  <title>IMG_20171109_215943 (1)</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/?attachment_id=1534</link>

<item>
  <title>How to digitally produce a long exposure picture without a tripod or ND filters</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2016/08/29/how-to-shoot-a-long-exposure-picture-without-a-tripod-or-nd-filters/</link>
  <pubDate>Mon, 29 Aug 2016 15:51:14 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=808</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Lets say you hiking in the Canadian wilderness and come across a fantastic waterfall. Of course you don't have a tripod with you because they are big, heavy and generally a drag. Long exposure shots of waterfalls are fantastic, while regular shots are mostly boring. What to do? Here is the solution.
<h3>Step 1 - Shooting</h3>
Set your camera to manual exposure time, aperture and white balance. Select a shooting mode that will allow you to take many sequential pictures. Frame your shot, and take 20 to 30 pictures. The more the better, but no need to go crazy. <strong>You can do this freehand</strong>. No need for a tripod, a big rock or anything else. Here are six sample pictures, you'll need more.

[gallery ids="821,822,823,824,825,826" columns="6" link="none"]

&nbsp;
<h3>Step2 - Layering</h3>
Fire up Photoshop, and load all the pictures into a single image with lots of layers. You can do this manually, or using the <strong>File &gt; Scripts &gt; Load files into a Stack</strong>option.

Select all the layers by clicking on them, or <strong>Select &gt; Select all layers</strong>, or <strong>Ctrl-Alt-A</strong>

Go to <strong>Edit &gt; Auto Align Layers</strong>and Choose <strong>Auto</strong>as the alignment mode.
<h3>Step 3-Stacking</h3>
Make sure all the layers are still selected. Go to<strong>Layer &gt; Smart Objects &gt; Convert toSmart Object</strong>

Now do<strong>Layer &gt; Smart Objects &gt; Stack Mode &gt; Mean</strong> and you'll get something like this:

<img class="alignnone  wp-image-848" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/08/img_9984-custom.jpg" alt="IMG_9984 (Custom).jpg" width="594" height="419" />

You can also experiment with different <strong>Stack Modes</strong> for different (and weird) results (Median also works well).

That's it! This also works for simulating very long night exposures and star trails, and much more. Have fun.

&nbsp;]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>Sculpting in VR</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2016/09/07/sculpting-in-vr/</link>
  <pubDate>Wed, 07 Sep 2016 21:18:59 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=863</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<img class="alignnone  wp-image-882" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/09/capture.png" alt="Capture.PNG" width="245" height="163" />

My <a href="https://lenagorelick.wordpress.com/">wife</a> sells 3D printed sterling silver jewelry (<a href="http://nunandnoot.com">Nn&amp;Noot</a>). She currently uses a combination of clay and digital sculpting tools to design her very organic pieces.

<img class="alignnone  wp-image-871" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2016/09/process.png?w=990" alt="process.png" width="563" height="100" />

We recently had the opportunity to try sculpting on VR, using the Vive and <a href="http://store.steampowered.com/app/479010/">Kodon</a>, which was the only VR sculpting app I could find. Kodon has a lot of promise, but it's very early stage and lacks some crucial features. I really hope they keep working on it and it matures into a productive tool. Anyway, it was seriously fun.

Here is a video of my wife in action.

http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=6Mn3l-tnt1U

&nbsp;

<item>
  <title>3D scanning using Agisoft Photoscan and an automated turn table</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2017/03/15/3d-scanning-using-agisoft-photoscan-and-an-automated-turn-table/</link>
  <pubDate>Wed, 15 Mar 2017 16:02:47 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=902</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[I'm working on a VR game and I needed a bunch of household "old-timey" objects. Since my 3D modelling skills are limited to CAD, I decided to 3D scan these items. Luckily I happen to have a collection of antique cameras, so these were good candidates. After much experimentation here is the setup I ended up with, and it works very well.
<h2>Software</h2>
You will need <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photogrammetry">photogrammetry </a>software, I used Agisoft Photoscan. They offer a 30 day fully functional <a href="http://www.agisoft.com/downloads/request-trial/">trial</a>, which is nice. You can also try the free and open source <a href="http://ccwu.me/vsfm/">VisualSFM</a>. It works well but is a bit tricky to setup and use.
<h2>Lighting</h2>
It is <em>crucial</em> that you have uniform and <strong>shadowless</strong> light. This can be achieved in several ways, with (expensive) studio lighting gear, reflectors, softboxes or simply shooting outdoors on a gray overcast day. I cleared out a small storage space in my basement and placed inside a single LED light pointing at the ceiling. Whatever works for you, just getthat light to be as uniform as possible.
<h2>Turn Table</h2>
While it's not mandatory to have an automated turn table it does make life<b>a lot</b>easier, and it's fairly simple to do. You will need:
<ul>
	<li><a href="https://www.arduino.cc/">Arduino</a>(or clone), I used the <a href="https://www.arduino.cc/en/Main/arduinoBoardNano">Nano</a>but any version would do.</li>
	<li>Stepper Motor</li>
	<li>Stepper Motor driver (I used the <a href="https://www.sparkfun.com/products/12779">EasyDriver</a>for this project)</li>
	<li>A<a href="http://www.ebay.com/sch/i.html?_from=R40&amp;_trksid=p2050601.m570.l1313.TR12.TRC2.A0.H0.Xarduino+relay.TRS0&amp;_nkw=arduino+relay&amp;_sacat=0">Relay Module</a>for Arduino (you can also use a transistor if you are feeling adventurous)</li>
	<li>A wired remote for your camera</li>
	<li>A lazy suzan turn table (I got mine from Goodwill for $2)</li>
	<li>12V power supply</li>
</ul>
Mount the turntable on a piece of wood, hook up the motor with some kind of wheel (I used Lego). There is some improvisation needed here, so be ready to tinker. Connect the EasyDriver to the Arduino (<a href="http://bildr.org/2011/06/easydriver/">here</a>is a nice guide). Hookup the relay to the arduino (like <a href="https://elementztechblog.wordpress.com/2014/07/21/interfacing-relay-boards-to-arduino/">this</a>).

Cut the wired remote and connect the leads to the relay. You might need to experiment with which wires to connect, there will probably be 3 wires in there to simulate half-press and full-press of the trigger button. You want full-press.

I wrote a very simple program to drive the table and trigger the camera, you can find it <a href="https://www.dropbox.com/s/tuzm8cd5ecgyppz/turnTableV2.ino?dl=0">here</a>. I opted for 52 exposures per rotation, and I usually shoot three angles, Low (looking slightly up), mid level and high (looking down at the object) for a total of 156 images.

Place something with a lot of texture on the turn table, like a magazine cutout. If you are using Agisoft, print and add a few markers (in Agisoft go to the Tools menu -&gt; markers -&gt; print markers). These help Agisoft orient the cameras properly.

<img class="alignnone  wp-image-974" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/03/img_20170315_102756.jpg" alt="IMG_20170315_102756.jpg" width="527" height="426" />
<h2>Camera</h2>
You'll want the best camera you can get your hands on. A DSLR is a must. Sorry, your iPhone just won't cut it for this application. Set your camera to fully manual, close the aperture to increase the <a href="https://petapixel.com/2015/03/17/this-online-depth-of-field-simulator-helps-you-wrap-your-mind-around-the-concept/">Depth Of Field</a>(I shoot at f11), set exposure time to whatever works (but keep it fixed) and ISO should be below 800. Set camera to manual focus and shoot <a href="https://digital-photography-school.com/raw-vs-jpeg/">RAW</a>. Slap on a polarizing filter if your object has shiny or reflective surfaces.
<h2>RAW Post-processing</h2>
Open the RAW files in your favorite RAW editing software. Bump up the shadows and pull down the highlights to make the image as "flat" as possible. Increase sharping a little, and add some noise removal. Crop if needed, export as PNG.

<img class="alignnone  wp-image-936" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/03/capture.png?w=604" alt="Capture.PNG" width="496" height="455" />
<h2>Agisoft</h2>
<ul>
	<li>Create a new project, add all the images</li>
	<li>If you have markers visible, do <strong>Tools-&gt;Markers-&gt;Detect Markers</strong></li>
	<li>Run <strong>Workflow-&gt;Align Cameras</strong> using "High" quality setting (this can take 10-20 minutes)</li>
	<li>Using the "Resize region" tool (in the toolbar) adjust the region of interest to include only your object (and not the turn table).</li>
	<li>Run <strong>Workflow-&gt;Build Dense Cloud</strong>using "High" quality setting (this will take 1-2 hours). I once tried "Ultra High" and it worked for 20 hours and then crashed with an "out of memory" error. I'll stick with "High".</li>
	<li>Run <strong>Workflow-&gt;BuildMesh</strong>. It's best to use high polygon count and then decimate.</li>
	<li>[Optional] Export the mesh, open it in Z-brush or <a href="http://www.meshlab.net/">MeshLab</a>, clean, simplify or resurface it. Close holes, deleted small floating noise, etc. Just make it nice. Then drag it back into Agisoft, and it will replace the mesh there.</li>
	<li>Run<strong>Workflow-&gt;Build texture</strong> with a 4k or 8k texture.</li>
	<li>Export Mesh, done.</li>
</ul>
<h2>Conclusion</h2>
3D scanning stuff is fun and frustrating. Some things work well, some don't. Reflective surfaces are difficult, also objects that are smooth and without detail will fail or generate noisy result. When it works though, it's stunning.

Here is my setup in action:
[youtube https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=cyfAwIQmTYc]

And the resulting scan:

https://sketchfab.com/models/1ef02c8fc2364d4c86836ae31a37d95a

And one more video, this time in VR and with a bunch of scanned objects:

https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=iyRHzWlZpiA

<item>
  <title>Increasing the HTC Vive FOV</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2017/04/15/increasing-the-htc-vive-fov/</link>
  <pubDate>Sat, 15 Apr 2017 15:10:57 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=1043</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Thisvery simple hack will dramatically increase your HTC Vive field-of-view. The Vive comes with two face covers packaged in the box, wide and narrow. I opted to hack the narrow one since I wasn't using it (I'm mr. big head).

<span style="color:#993300;">Warning</span>- If you need glasses to use the Vive this might not work for you.

Here is the FOV increase I got (approx), compared to the stock wide face cover.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-1065" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/04/fovmod.png?w=890" alt="fovmod.png" width="445" height="389" />

The stock "narrow" face cover:

<img class="alignnone  wp-image-1048" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/04/narrow.jpg" alt="narrow" width="476" height="278" />

Take a sharpknife and carefully slice off 2/3 of the foam. It can be abit tricky to get it nice and even and it's a lot easier if you have another pair of handsto help hold it down (preferably a pair of hands that are connected to person).

<img class="alignnone  wp-image-1049" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/04/narrow2.jpg" alt="narrow2" width="491" height="286" />

Aaaaaand.. that's it. Here is the shaved-down face cover on my vive, which is also sporting the <a href="http://www.thingiverse.com/thing:1620362">welder headset mod</a> (highly recommended, if you can't wait for the deluxe strap or want a cheaper solution). I didn't notice any difference in comfort. Have fun!

<img class="alignnone  wp-image-1046" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/04/narrow3.jpg" alt="Narrow3" width="501" height="293" />

&nbsp;

&nbsp;]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>Unboxing and first look at the new Microsoft "Mixed Reality" VR headset</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2017/07/29/unboxing-and-first-look-at-the-new-microsoft-mixed-reality-vr-headset/</link>
  <pubDate>Sat, 29 Jul 2017 19:37:51 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=1094</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<a href="http://packet39.com/">Packet39</a>(my company) was lucky enough to receive a developer kit of the new MS "Mixed Reality" (ie, VR) headset, made by Acer. Here is a quick unboxing and review. Please keep in mind this is a developer model, and things might change before it's public release.
<h3>Mixed Reality or VR?</h3>
First thing I want to get out of the way is Microsoft's baffling decision of calling this headset "Mixed Reality". It's not a "Mixed Reality" headset by any means I can think of. It's a VR headset, nothing more, nothing less. There is no real-world 3D overlay, optically or with the cameras.

EDIT:I just discovered that these new "mixed reality" headsets do not 3D scan or map the environment. They use two sensors to figure out headset movement, and that's it. Those sensors in the front of the headset are not cameras (although I'm still not clear on what they are exactly) Thus, the "mixed reality" label doesn't make any sense IMHO.
<h3>Unboxing</h3>
Nothing exciting or interesting here, the headset came in a very simple package that consisted of the headset itself and a small note. No accessories, bags, mounts or anything else.

[gallery ids="1114,1113" type="rectangular"]

&nbsp;
<h3>The Headset</h3>
The headset is surprisingly lightweight (380 grams / 0.8 pound) and feels cheaply constructed, which is not surprising because<strong>it is</strong> cheap ($300 USD). There is no IPD adjustment or focus knob (like on the Vive).

[gallery ids="1110,1109,1107,1106" type="rectangular"]

The cable is USB 3.0 and HDMI and is about 3.3m (10f) long. There is no breakout box and it doesn't require external power (which is nice). The headstrap is a simple loop that goes on the forehead and the back of the head, and is adjustable with "press to slide" kind of locking ratchet. Not very smooth or elegant, but gets the job done. It's all made of rigid plastic, no fabrics or stretchy materials, with some padding on the forehead. Since the headset is so lightweight it works out ok.

[gallery ids="1108,1112,1111" type="rectangular"]

The foam is detachable (velcro) and there is a small IR proximity sensor inside. The headset is hinged and can be flipped up in a somewhat awkward fashion. An audio jack is hidden on the left side, which can be used for wired headphones (not included).

[gallery ids="1116,1115" type="rectangular"]

&nbsp;
<h3>Initial setup</h3>
I plugged in the headset and windows immediately downloaded and installed the drivers, and launched the "Mixed Reality Portal". The first step was to enter my height, so the headset can detect the floor. The next step was standing in the middle of the room, then tracing out my play area perimeter, by holding the headset at waist level and walking around the room.

This actually took several attempts. The first one wasn't good enough (not sure why). The second was too small, which is amusing given the rather short cable. My computer is not directly by my table, and I also have a treadmill desk (yeah, it's as crazy as it sounds), so I had to move back a bit. Even with the cable fully extended, the biggest area I could trace was too small. I had to move the computer and try again. Unlike with the SteamVR room setup, the boundary doesn't have to be rectangular.

[gallery ids="1102,1103,1104" type="rectangular"]

&nbsp;
<h3>Tracking</h3>
The tracking is good. It's not as good as the SteamVR/Vive lighthouse tracking system, but it's impressively good for an inside-out tracking system with no external sensors or transmitters. The headset supports full room-scale, 6DOF tracking, with an occasional jitter / flicker when you move your head quickly. Again, the Vive / Rift is better, but this comes very close.
<h3>Display</h3>
The display is a mixed bag. On the upside, the resolution is higher than the Vive/Rift (1440x1440 pixels vs Vive's 1080x1200), and it's noticeable. Sharper image, considerably less screen door affect. However, optically it falls short. The sweet spot is small and the edge smear is very pronounced. Also, it has a distinct "viewing the world through binoculars" feel to it, as you can clearly see a circular edge.It is very hard to capture images or video through the headset, but here are a few. Please do not judge the display quality by these:

[gallery ids="1143,1144,1145" type="rectangular"]
<h3></h3>
EDIT: Interestingly, the lens seems to be a hybrid, smooth on the outside and Fresnel'ed on the inside.
<h3><img class="  wp-image-1153 aligncenter" style="font-size:16px;font-weight:normal;" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/07/img_20170729_184000.jpg?w=250" alt="IMG_20170729_184000.jpg" width="197" height="162" /></h3>
<h3>Chaperon</h3>
Just like SteamVR, it shows up when you are close to it.

https://youtu.be/toUJw4ulkj8

&nbsp;
<h3>Controls / User Interface</h3>
The headset will get tracked controllers, but those are not released yet. For now, it's voice or mouse / keyboard interface and it does that in an interesting way. Here is a video that shows the gist of it, it looks cumbersome but it actually works.. OK (ish). Still, using a keyboard (that I can't see) and a mouse (that I can't see) in VR is not my cup of tea.

https://youtu.be/W5FYwMhUZoM

&nbsp;

That's all I have for now, I'm going to dive into the software SDK and write some more about that very soon.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>3D modeling curved objects with a flatbed scanner</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2017/08/04/3d-modeling-curved-objects-with-a-flatbed-scanner/</link>
  <pubDate>Fri, 04 Aug 2017 01:20:05 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=1157</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Here is a neat trick that can save a lot of time when modeling objects.

Say you have an object that you want to model with high fidelity. And also say you are planning to attach a Vive tracker to this object and swing it around in VR (coming soon). Like so:

<img class="aligncenter  wp-image-1172" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/08/img_20170803_210218.jpg?w=454" alt="IMG_20170803_210218.jpg" width="463" height="261" />

How would you go about measuring the curves?
You can use one of these, maybe?
<img class=" aligncenter" src="https://is.alicdn.com/img/pb/514/956/248/1273732872376_hz-myalibaba-web17_1554.jpg" width="186" height="106" />

Or these?

<img class=" aligncenter" src="http://www.trick-tools.com/common/images/products/large/rg-3600.jpg" width="142" height="136" />

Surly there is an easier way? Yup. There is. The humble flatbed scanner. Available for pennies at your local second-hand store or ebay. Place your object on the scanner, like so:

<img class="SzDcob aligncenter" src="https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/_mG_eUnWG1Jf1FN0yhH3eQO4zvecAvdayh9PU95fwmNKIZXC990GTlb5LQtOwkqzc4z4BofNM5vR8O5QHVGY9NP76fMi7OhQSkg2OUaN0tT7U8pyxadrpgqaGZZExAHroe9ddtdT18AnOHg5rKX0sigjN4PXktSON30zeKSZhetuieTLaWanGX9D5gAlEog9YncH1JfR2wY5vLsBAtloPlUvULlFggtdlgwyan_3XrGYZJo5O5_tgff5pIVSWJwqq8l9czozG0W92RE5b6OcpYtzLumsAwy67LhyKeRGqxtGjILRqwNW7nR5X2rW0twAgyu_zjOBGRGWmtlg84A-j166QP4ARS0yRRcx2HYRFGP0DRLlT7p0zu1p8O8yBTBlAXmxW4jvMDo1tRnBhwiuxNGkSsJDtcJGD1iJQdVHV4iA_WDorw_tsJwPnR3u-fujPqYXxrNefDMdHI06Hq2t0_7i0m665RA2r3QF_o7XirXSzPbAqMm5Uvn4UmsIFXdnb3Eo9oNlroSQtiMaOQtzL-6rep1nxYprQ9jreaPAk01aM4y2a2wokNii9fC_no7w0gDCcKj7j32LnI1RuoAatMXANCrP63UM13OwhIdhYSOsaO5PHXCdkjPv=w2471-h1390-no" width="461" height="259" />

Object is too big? Scan it in part, assemble automatically using a panorama stitching tool, or manually in any photo editor. It's fairly easy. You'll end up with a scan that looks like this:

<img class="  wp-image-1180 aligncenter" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/08/raquet.jpg?w=774" alt="raquet.jpg" width="532" height="235" />

It's going to look bad. Fuzzy with a lot of color noise and crazy pixels. That's fine. We don't care about picture quality, we just need the curves. I also scanned the cross section of the handle, because I couldn't find my caliper and for the sake of completeness. Now I can say that everything about this model was flatbed scanned.

<img class="SzDcob aligncenter" src="https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/CmpVOlntxFMTQaCvdHIzqV88fpjYtHPoeR1bTLAkC9wwGmjUd9pNwTN0ySr38R4HaxfS2YaL8g4Mx3smMIKZfNMPbhnRub3jnghmK37zTpZ-Duvxp9GToRcewot2qKGSvbxa97t6St0Nj8btqQVCq4ciQwpDW7lmrXz9iQYOXB5rAuH6VNcB0YFNZ5deWH6BkRsylG2a-XRCAgAZG5hxx5anArA1BlUMY5ecsMeeX0Rr_z4zBmeFQ7rrY9T12ggerSHSm6g6C39fxkdQ_dnxPd1px0shVnaOw4h-nlcZ5JCGw8SoUaU3uOpscJ6IRMiCEV-qviUKUEDG0hUNSOKmcv2QZYyfrcuYRCXvAct6lYAi_EPsM645wH-3b0zTE2L2CVkcKYwZ3ok9B6ZtTVxfvUBx-di1MWeNMRcCIXQ1eI6aG-W0tMqe79sv74qRzK7afiUqspWAtPGjzSKbYXpAIwdn9x3gPAKv8HS8KKE9mhVIllkETP1hMc4e8lFbB91RiCvHuq8Kf9K20VkWIvNyfHUqnFaBA2SlTUa0_aUST9wctp4XzBlSTMG4rQDTF6DYwI3WJsGowGxwOjTJEmmtmicKl8wQe0RIbSpOc6MSSMfpHz2Geb15acfz=w2471-h1390-no" width="455" height="256" />
The images should come out of the scanner exactly to-scale, meaning you can import them directly into your 3D modeling software of choice, trace the lines and have a to-scale replica of your object. First I traced the curves:

<img class="aligncenter  wp-image-1192" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/08/capture.png?w=608" alt="Capture.PNG" width="477" height="265" />

Then some sweeps, extrudes and lofts, and voila, one world-scale racquetball racquet is reading for texturing:

<img class="aligncenter  wp-image-1199" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/08/capture2.png?w=836" alt="Capture2.PNG" width="487" height="265" />
Coming up soon, attaching the Vive tracker and bring the racquet into Unity.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>Teardown of the Acer / Microsoft "Mixed Reality" VR headset</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2017/08/04/teardown-of-the-acer-microsoft-mixed-reality-vr-headset/</link>
  <pubDate>Fri, 04 Aug 2017 21:38:29 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=1214</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[I wanted to see whats inside the new Acer VR headset that my company (<a href="http://Packet39.com">Packet39</a>) received a few days ago. So I took it apart and documented the process.

<img class="  wp-image-1242 aligncenter" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/08/img_20170804_161531.jpg" alt="IMG_20170804_161531" width="408" height="274" />

Velcro comes off, then 6 small screws. Then the plastic "face cover" detaches, and the headband with it.

&nbsp;

&nbsp;

[gallery ids="1241,1240,1239" type="columns"]

&nbsp;

The two lens holders (or maybe protectors, as they don't actually hold anything) pop off. Four more screws and then another plastic cover comes off (not easily though).

&nbsp;

&nbsp;

[gallery ids="1238,1236,1237" type="rectangular"]

&nbsp;

Now the IR proximity sensor and audio jack are exposed.

&nbsp;

&nbsp;

[gallery ids="1236,1235,1234,1233" type="rectangular"]

&nbsp;

One more screw and the audio jack comes out.

&nbsp;

&nbsp;

[gallery ids="1232,1231" type="rectangular"]

&nbsp;

Four additional screws and the LCD+lenses module comes off. I didn't open that one, because they are usually hermetically sealed and I don't have a clean room to put it back together. The LCD module is connected to the main board by two ribbon cables, and the IR sensor is the third.

&nbsp;

&nbsp;

[gallery ids="1230,1229,1228" type="rectangular"]

&nbsp;

Back side of the main board, and the QR code / serial number.

&nbsp;

&nbsp;

&nbsp;

[gallery ids="1227,1225" type="rectangular"]

&nbsp;

The two cameras, each have their own ribbon cable and a fancy cable protector. I peel off the shielding but there is no label on the camera itself. Sadly, I still have no idea what kind of cameras these are. Each camera has a flat and flexible heatsink glued to the back, that extends to the sides of the headset.

&nbsp;

&nbsp;

[gallery ids="1226,1224" type="rectangular"]

&nbsp;

There is nothing else in the headset, no additional sensors or emitters.

&nbsp;

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-1223" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/08/img_20170804_163813.jpg" alt="IMG_20170804_163813" width="5168" height="2550" />

&nbsp;

Here is the front side of the main board, with some closeups on the chip numbers. I haven't looked these up yet, if you know what they do please comment on this blog post.

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-1218" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/08/img_20170804_163911.jpg" alt="IMG_20170804_163911" width="5213" height="2932" />

&nbsp;

&nbsp;

[gallery ids="1222,1221,1220,1219" type="rectangular"]

<img class="alignnone size-full wp-image-1317" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/08/unnamed.jpg" alt="unnamed" width="3888" height="2592" />

Everything was put back together in reverse order without much difficulty. Headset still works, so great success. I hope you find this useful.]]></content:encoded>

    <wp:comment_id>301</wp:comment_id>
    <wp:comment_author><![CDATA[brianpeiris]]></wp:comment_author>
    <wp:comment_author_email>brianpeiris@gmail.com</wp:comment_author_email>
    <wp:comment_author_url>http://brianpeiris.wordpress.com/</wp:comment_author_url>
    <wp:comment_author_IP>24.4.121.11</wp:comment_author_IP>
    <wp:comment_date>2017-08-29 22:18:26</wp:comment_date>
    <wp:comment_date_gmt>2017-08-29 22:18:26</wp:comment_date_gmt>
    <wp:comment_content><![CDATA[Thanks for the teardown! I've tracked down the main chips on the front side of the board. I'm pretty sure the back side of the board is mostly incidental power circuitry, but I'm only an armchair electrical engineer.

CYUSB3304
Cypress USB 3.0 hub.
http://www.cypress.com/documentation/datasheets/cyusb330xcyusb331xcyusb332xcyusb230x-hx3-usb-30-hub

STM32F103C8
ST ARM Cortex-M3 74MHz microcontroller (includes USB capabilities)
http://www.st.com/en/microcontrollers/stm32f103c8.html

LIFMD6K
Lattice LIF-MD6000 FPGA Probably used to combine the stereo cameras into one CSI-2 image stream but it could also do some image processing.
http://www.latticesemi.com/Products/FPGAandCPLD/CrossLink.aspx

CYUSB3064
Cypress CSI-2 to USB bridge controller.
http://www.cypress.com/documentation/datasheets/cyusb306x-ez-usb-cx3-mipi-csi-2-superspeed-usb-bridge-controller

ALC4040
Realtek USB audio chip.
Not listed on Realtek's site

ANX7678
Analogix SlimPort Probably an hdmi to display port converter.
Not listed on the Analogix site

ANX7530
Analogix SlimPort Display port receiver. Analogix made this chip for VR. It outputs to two displays.
http://www.analogix.com/en/products/dp-mipi-converters/anx7530

25Q80DVSIG 
Winbond 8Mbit serial flash memory.
http://www.winbond.com/resource-files/w25q80dv_revf_02112015.pdf]]></wp:comment_content>

<item>
  <title>Final Thoughts about the MS/Acer VR headset (I refuse to call it Mixed Reality)</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2017/08/06/final-thoughts-about-the-msacer-vr-headset-i-refuse-to-call-it-mixed-reality/</link>
  <pubDate>Sun, 06 Aug 2017 18:37:55 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=1283</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<img class="  wp-image-1308 aligncenter" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/08/20170806_150202.jpg" alt="20170806_150202" width="308" height="393" />

About a week ago I got an Microsoft / Acer VR headset, which MS insists on called "Mixed Reality". I <a href="https://callmevice.com/2017/07/29/unboxing-and-first-look-at-the-new-microsoft-mixed-reality-vr-headset/">reviewed</a> it, I took it <a href="https://callmevice.com/2017/08/04/teardown-of-the-acer-microsoft-mixed-reality-vr-headset/">apart.</a>I made some tests in Unity3D. I emailed MS support.Here are my final thoughts:
<h2><b><u>Price</u></b></h2>
<div>It's cheap. Currently priced at $300. While a few weeks ago I would have considered this incredibly cheap, in light of the Rift's recent price cut, the cheapness factor has diminished somewhat.</div>
<div></div>
<div></div>
<h2></h2>
<h2><b><u>Comfort</u></b></h2>
<div>The unit is light (which is good) but feels really cheaply constructed. The strap is minimalistic and not very comfortable, consisting almost entirely of hard injection molded plastic. The forehead rest has some cushioning, thank goodness.</div>
<div></div>
<div></div>
<h2></h2>
<h2><b><u>Display</u></b></h2>
<div>The resolution is great, sharp image with lots of detail that just pops right at you. Screen door effect is still there though. It's hard to compare it to the Rift or Vive. I feel that it's less pronounced, but that's entirely subjective. The optics and field-of-view are noticeably inferior to Vive/Rift, with the Acer having a smaller "sweet spot", lots of blurriness in the borders and a distinct "tunnel vision" effect.</div>
<div></div>
<h2></h2>
<h2><b><u>Setup</u></b></h2>
<div>There is almost none, which is fantastic. The headset plugs into the computer and that's it. There are no cameras or lighthouses to install. It doesn't even require external power. The minimal spec is very low, but I haven't tested how well it functions on a low-end system. I suspect the experience will be greatly diminished.</div>
<div></div>
<h2></h2>
<h2><b><u>Tracking</u></b></h2>
<div>Tracking is good, not as good as the Vive (it's a bit jittery), but better than any mobile VR headset. Positional tracking is solid. Interestingly, the headset doesn't function in the dark. Turn off the lights and it will lose tracking.</div>
<div></div>
<h2></h2>
<h2><b><u>Content</u></b></h2>
<div>There is none. I found a demo for the Hololens, and that's about it. You can "inject" regular 2D UWP apps into your 3D virtual space, as floating screens. While this is really cool to have on a Hololens, I don't see the point of doing it in VR. Some people might find a use for this feature, to me it's not very alluring. The display resolution is good, but not good enough to read small text and certainly not as good as my 4K monitor. Also, normal "desktop" software can't be injected, which rules out 99% of the apps I normally use.</div>
<div></div>
<h2></h2>
<h2><b><u>Mixed Reality</u></b></h2>
<div>Unlike the Hololens, these new "MR" headsets do not have 3D cameras and do not map the environment around them. Furthermore, the two cameras on the front are used for tracking and nothing else. They are not currently accessible via the SDK, and there is no way to get a video feed from them. This is confirmed by a MS rep over email.</div>
<div></div>
<div>Microsoft is pushing new terminology of a "mixed reality spectrum", it's just that these headsets are waaaay over on the VR side of things. They "mix" 0% real and 100% virtual. Honestly, I'm disappointed. I was hoping for some kind of poor-man's Hololens, but it's really not. If the Acer is a MR headset, then by the same logic so is this:</div>
<div></div>
<div><img class=" aligncenter" src="http://cdn6.bigcommerce.com/s-d8bzk61/images/stencil/1280x1280/products/2528/6970/Pyramex_Intruder_Safety_Glasses_with_Clear_Lens__16824.1474037730.jpg?c=2" width="304" height="171" /></div>
<h2></h2>
<h2><b><u>Development</u></b></h2>
<div>The Acer headsets requires Unity 2017.2 Beta to build and run custom apps. No other version will work. Being a beta, 2017.2 is very unstable and can be frustrating to work with. I spent a few hours making a simple "hello world" app, consisting of a few floating textured cubes, and I had to restart my system twice. The headset stopped responding completely after the first build attempt. The second time I got a very weird "double vision" effect, where my app was visible twice inside the VR world, one in front and another in the back. I can't explain that one, because it didn't happen again after a restart.</div>
<div>Overall it works, but it's finicky and unstable at the moment.</div>
<div></div>
<h2></h2>
<h2><b><u>UWP</u></b></h2>
<div>Being Microsoft, the headset will only run UWP apps. For anyone hoping for an OpenVR bridge or driver, this is a problem. UWP is all about layering, security and sandboxing. It might be very tricky to convince this headset to run OpenVR or SteamVR applications.</div>
<div></div>
<div>EDIT:</div>
<div></div>
<div>MS announced that these new headsets will support SteamVR, however, this will not happen at launch (Fall 2017). Work on this has just begun, and there is no release date yet.</div>
<h2></h2>
<h2><b><u>Conclusion</u></b></h2>
<div>I love the simplicity of this device, the ease of setup and the display resolution. I don't like the optics and being stuck with UWP (for now?). I intend on using mine as a mobile demo tool, at least for the time being.</div>
<div></div>
<div>Is it worth buying?I don't think so. Not yet anyway. For an extra $100 you could get an Oculus Rift, with two controllers and a lot of supported content, apps and games.</div>
&nbsp;

&nbsp;

&nbsp;]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>VR for Industry</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2017/09/19/vr-for-industry/</link>
  <pubDate>Tue, 19 Sep 2017 21:18:52 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=1333</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<b><u>Geiger Counter</u></b>

http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=htp0rUHSunw

Training and testing on the usage of a hand-held radiation detector.

This is a pilot project for the nuclear power plant in Pickering, Ontario. An operator places a virtual radiation source on the table and the trainee has to find it. Only the operator can see the source on screen. In VR, this test can be conducted in an office or even at home, without expensive equipment and without exposing trainees to a radiation source.

<b><u>Crane Demo</u></b>

http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=doGPd8wYFNs

This is a demo of combining a virtual device (the crane) with a real-life controller (the levers). The user can see the levers in VR and operate the controller with their hands. The virtual and physical controllers are mapped 1:1 (position and scale). It is also possible to show the user's hands in VR, for easier orientation.

<b><u>Audio Testing Room</u></b>

http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=vY0xsmCPbnQ

This was is a test developed for the audiology department at the University of Western Ontario. A user is presented with a VR room that includes several audio sources. The sources can be moved in 3D space and their volume / reach adjusted. This VR setup could potentially replace audio testing rooms, which have dozens of custom speakers and cost upwards of $100k.]]></content:encoded>
  <excerpt:encoded><![CDATA[]]></excerpt:encoded>
  <wp:post_id>1333</wp:post_id>
  <wp:post_date>2017-09-19 21:18:52</wp:post_date>
  <wp:post_date_gmt>2017-09-19 21:18:52</wp:post_date_gmt>
  <wp:comment_status>open</wp:comment_status>
  <wp:ping_status>open</wp:ping_status>
  <wp:post_name>vr-for-industry</wp:post_name>
  <wp:status>publish</wp:status>
  <wp:post_parent>0</wp:post_parent>
  <wp:menu_order>0</wp:menu_order>
  <wp:post_type>post</wp:post_type>
  <wp:post_password/>

  <content:encoded><![CDATA[My company (Packet39.com) is working a lot with the manufacturing and power industry. I wanted to see how I can take a physical controller and bring it into the VR environment. I modeled the joystick and mapped it 1:1 with the real device.

<item>
  <title>How to troll Google Maps on a national level</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2017/10/03/how-to-troll-google-maps-on-a-national-level/</link>
  <pubDate>Tue, 03 Oct 2017 12:37:34 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=1356</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[If you happen to look at Israel on Google maps, on <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yom_Kippur">Yom Kippur</a>, you would notice something interesting. The entire country is red with traffic. These are no ordinary traffic jams.

<img class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-1368" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/10/capture.png?w=650" alt="Capture.PNG" width="325" height="511" />

So what is going on? Something weird happens on Yum Kippur in Israel. For 24 hours, from dusk to dusk, nobody drives. The religious and atheists alike take a break from their cars. The roads and highways are empty and everyone takes to the streets. You'll see kids biking on deserted highways, people just hanging out in the middle of an intersection. It feels post-apocalyptic and it's amazing. The silence hits you like a wall.

<img class=" aligncenter" src="https://static.timesofisrael.com/www/uploads/2015/09/F141003FFDS008-e1442908153128.jpg" alt="Image result for israel yom kippur night picture" />

&nbsp;

Of course many of these people have cellphones, and none of them are moving very fast, hence, Google thinks it's a country-spanning traffic jam. And that's how you troll Google maps in style. I wish every country would adopt this tradition.

Hell, I would make it a monthly event.

&nbsp;]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>Microsoft Motion Controllers - Unboxing and first look</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2017/10/10/microsoft-motion-controller-unboxing-and-first-look/</link>
  <pubDate>Tue, 10 Oct 2017 21:01:17 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=1382</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Hooray, I just got my Microsoft Motion Controllers, to pair with my Acer "MixedReality" headset (which can't actually do MR in any way). See my review <a href="https://callmevice.com/2017/08/06/final-thoughts-about-the-msacer-vr-headset-i-refuse-to-call-it-mixed-reality/">here</a>and <a href="https://callmevice.com/2017/07/29/unboxing-and-first-look-at-the-new-microsoft-mixed-reality-vr-headset/">here</a>.

So, Are they any good?
<h3>Short Answer</h3>
Tracking is superb. Build quality is <strong>horrible</strong>.
<h3>Long Answer</h3>
I received two controllers in a plain cardboard box devoid or any marking, text or symbols.

<img class="  wp-image-1394 aligncenter" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/10/img_20171010_152013.jpg" alt="IMG_20171010_152013.jpg" width="453" height="255" />

The box contains two controllers and two pairs of AA batteries, and nothing else.
<img class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-1399" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/10/img_20171010_152042-e1507667272816.jpg?w=856" alt="img_20171010_152042.jpg" width="428" height="288" />

Put in the batteries, hold the Windows button to turn on, and pesto. The first surprise. It uses white visible light LED. For some reason I thought these would be IR.

<img class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-1404" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/10/img_20171010_152334.jpg?w=746" alt="IMG_20171010_152334.jpg" width="373" height="210" />

&nbsp;

Moving on to the software, running Mixed Reality Portal greeted me with this screen:

<img class="  wp-image-1414 aligncenter" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/10/capture1.png" alt="Capture.PNG" width="404" height="338" />

After a bit of digging around I found that the battery compartment hides a secret button, holding it down for a few seconds puts in the controller into pairing mode. You'll need a Bluetooth capable computer (dongle not included). What isn't documented anywhere is that the pass code is 0000. Yeah, I guessed it. On my third attempt.

<img class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-1412" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/10/img_20171010_153436.jpg?w=780" alt="IMG_20171010_153436.jpg" width="390" height="219" />
<h3>The Good Part</h3>
The tracking is surprisingly good. It's really spot-on, HTC vive quality, as long as the controller is within view of the headset cameras. The FOV is 180 degrees horizontal but only about 100 degrees vertical. In fact, when moving up, the controller will sometimes lose tracking <strong>while it's still in the headset virtual view</strong> (because the cameras are tilted down).

When the controller is outside the camera view, it will freeze in position but will still track orientation.

There is a slight lag in this video, but that lag is only in the screen display. The headset view is lag-free.

https://youtu.be/Ejbkwbjn8mI

&nbsp;
<h3>The Bad Part</h3>
The build quality is.. how to put it gently.. cheap-plasticy-chinese-knockoff kind of quality. Here, watch this:

https://youtu.be/d-eZ3V2J4GI

&nbsp;

<strong>Trigger</strong>: It's binary but offers no click, no resistance, no feedback when you press it. It feels like a $5 RC car controller

<strong>Touchpad</strong>: Up, down, left and right all have a different "clickiness" to them. Different sound, different feel, different pressure required to trigger. UP is very "clicky", DOWN barely registers.

<strong>Thumb stick</strong>: Movement is OK, but also has a cheap feel to it. Clicking on it generates this nasty grinding sensation (clearly audible in the video). Both of my controllers exhibit this problem, but one is much more pronounced than the other. It really is horrible.

<strong>Buttons</strong>: The main "windows" button and the side button are both fine, but the smaller menu button doesn't register any click sensation or noise. It also doesn't do anything in the Mixed Reality Portal. I'm not sure if it's just broken (on both controllers), or if it's designed to be a "silent" button, or what. Actually, I don't care. It's terrible as well.
<h3>Conclusion</h3>
I was genuinely surprised how well the controllers track in space, and the headset tracking has also improved since I last tried it. It seems the latest Windows update has refined the tracking algorithms. When moving the controller sideways and down you can to reach pretty far before it loses tracking. Moving it up is problematic, as it goes out of camera view before it even leaves your virtual view.

The build quality is well below the standard I was expecting, and on par with cheap knockoff XBOX controllers.
<h4>About the author</h4>
Shachar "Vice" Weis is a software developer freelancer and the CTO / Founder of <a href="http://packet39.com">Packet39.com</a>, a software house that builds custom VR applications for the manufacturing and power industry.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>I installed a TPCast, was blown away, and then went back to tethered. A very subjective review.</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2017/10/21/i-installed-a-tpcast-was-blown-away-and-then-went-back-to-tethered-a-very-subjective-review/</link>
  <pubDate>Sat, 21 Oct 2017 12:54:20 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=1472</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[A few days ago I had the opportunity to grab a TPCast and install it on my home office Vive. Here are my thoughts:
<ul>
	<li>Lots of parts, clunky. Battery+holder, receiver, transmitter, router (why?)</li>
	<li>Setup was easier than expected. Pretty much plug in everything, install the TPCast software.</li>
	<li>Had some issues that went away after a reboot</li>
	<li>Exhilarating feeling of freedom without the tether. Mind blowing really.</li>
	<li>Very quickly realized I have nowhere to keep the battery. I usually VR in my underwear</li>
	<li>Transmitter makes a very annoying squeal / buzz when it's working</li>
	<li>I see a green bar at the very edge of peripheral vision on the right side. I <a href="https://callmevice.com/2017/04/15/increasing-the-htc-vive-fov/">increased my FOV</a>on my Vive by using a thin facepad. With the stock facepad the green bar is not visible, but it's really hard to go back to stock FOV.</li>
	<li>I did not see a reduction in image quality or lag</li>
	<li>I did notice some jitterness with motion-intensive games like <a href="http://store.steampowered.com/app/517130/Soundboxing/">SoundBoxing</a>. I can't put my finger on exactly what is the difference, but it's not as smooth as tethered. But again, only with very active games.</li>
	<li>The Vive microphone is not supported, nor is the headset camera or extra USB port.</li>
</ul>
I'm now back to good old tether. Why? It's just.. clunky. A collection of small annoyances. The battery in my pocket. The annoying buzz. The green bar glowing just outside my view. The extra tripod for the transmitter and extra cables for the router. Having to charge my headset. None of these is a huge deal, but put together.. I decided to go back to tethered.

&nbsp;

<strong>EDIT - Nov 2, 2017:</strong>

The great people of Opentpcast are working to solve some of these issues, I understand that the microphone is now working, and the camera very soon (for owners of the newer batches of Vives). Also they managed to improve tracking and reduce jitters. Check it out here:<a href="https://github.com/OpenTPCast/Docs">https://github.com/OpenTPCast/Docs</a>

In addition, I've seen unconfirmed reports that the US version of TPCast eliminates the annoying transmitter whine. I'll update this post when more information becomes available.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title>The Accommodation-Vergence conflict and how it affects your kids (and yourself)</title>
  <link>http://callmevice.com/2017/12/25/the-accommodation-vergence-conflict-and-how-it-affects-your-kids-and-yourself/</link>
  <pubDate>Mon, 25 Dec 2017 16:08:06 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>shacharweis</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://callmevice.com/?p=1667</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<h2>Introduction</h2>
Remember that first time you put on a VR headset? You were immersed in a virtual world and (hopefully) it was breath taking. Then, if you were in VR for a good amount of tine, when you took off the headset the real world felt... weird. Not right. Something odd about it. That feeling is very common for first-timers and usually goes away within a few minutes. That weirdness is your brain and eyes re-adjusting and switching back to their normal way of operating, after being forced to work in a very unusual manner.

So what is going on?

It's common knowledge that depth perception stems from the fact that we have two eyes, and we can infer depth from the displacement of objects between the two images. Large displacement (parallax) means close object, small or no displacement means far away object. This is true, but there much more to it. There are in fact a whole bunch of <a href="http://www.hitl.washington.edu/projects/knowledge_base/virtual-worlds/EVE/III.A.1.c.DepthCues.html">cues</a> that we use to infer depth. Lets talk about two of these.
<h2>Accommodation</h2>
Eyes are a marvel of engineering. Camera lenses have multiple optical elements, some are movable. To change focus, the elements move in relation to one another. Eyes have only a single element (the lens), but unlike cameras, our lens is soft and squishy. To change focus, special muscles pull or relax the lens, changing it's shape.

<img class=" aligncenter" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Focus_in_an_eye.svg/800px-Focus_in_an_eye.svg.png" width="491" height="166" />

&nbsp;

This is called the Accommodation reflex and it's an important depth cue for objects that are close to us (less than 10 meters / 30 feet).
<h2>Convergence</h2>
Our eyes can move independently, and when looking at an object they converge on it.
<img class="  wp-image-1668 aligncenter" src="https://lazygeckoblog.files.wordpress.com/2017/12/untitled-2.png" alt="Untitled-2.png" width="416" height="250" />

Again, this is another depth cue.
<h2>The Conflict</h2>
In the real world, Accommodation and Vergence are<strong>always</strong> in sync. VR is a different story. In a VR headset the screen is in a fixed distance from your eyes, usually around 3 meters (9 feet). The actual screen is of course much closer, but there are some optics in the headset to make it appear as if it's 3 meters away. Your eyes remain focused on the screen<strong>all the time</strong>. If a virtual object is a 20cm away, your eyes are<strong>converging</strong>on a spot that is 20cm away but they are <strong>focusing</strong> (accommodating) on the screen which is 3 meters away. This is totally wrong and never happens anywhere except VR headsets.

&nbsp;

<img class=" aligncenter" src="http://informationdisplay.org/portals/informationdisplay/issues/2012/03/art9/GIFS/fig2.jpg" alt="fig2" />

If you have a VR headset you can easily test this. Go into a VR environment and look at something on the horizon or far away. Now hold up the controller to your face. Note how both the controller and the horizon are in perfect focus. This is optically impossible and your brain is actually freaking out over it.

But wait! Surely 3D movies / TVs also have this problem, and they have been around for a long time. This is correct. However, the big difference is that 3D displays don't block out the real world and are usually far away from the user. They also cause this conflict, but to a<em> much lesser</em> degree.
<h2>Panic</h2>
As stated, the Accommodation-Vergence conflict is the reason why the real world feels weird after being in VR. It's also the main cause of eye-strain / headaches, and some people are more sensitive than others. I've seen reports of VR users losing depth perception for hours, and even days, after a long VR session. While in adults this is probably temporary, it does raise concerns in regards to children.

How does prolonged exposure to the Accommodation-Vergence conflict affect a developing visual system (ie, kids)? <strong>Nobody knows.</strong> There's no real data or studies on this topic, which brings me to the most important conclusion of this article:

<strong>Limit children's exposure to VR</strong>. Until we know more, it's best to err on the side of caution. Short exposure is<em>probably</em> fine, but don't let them spend hours in there, and insist on frequent breaks.

What about VR in education? Yes, this is a big problem. On one hand, VR can be an amazing tool in education, on the other, it also might cause permanent vision problems. Moderation is key, and if you are an educator I will lave this decision in your hands.

I'm aware of this <a href="http://n3.sinaimg.cn/sina_vr/647a35e2/20170612/YingWenBaoGao.pdf">study</a>, which showed a small improvement in vision when they tested a small group of kids for a short period of time. However, they did not test depth perception, which is the main area of concern.
<h2>Don't Panic</h2>
The good news is that theAccommodation-Vergence conflict is solvable, and the solution is <a href="http://lightfield-forum.com/light-field-camera-prototypes/nvidia-near-eye-light-field-display/">lightfield</a> technology. I won't go into much detail here, lightfields are damn complicated, but I will say what they solve the conflict and will allow for multiple planes of focus and more natural eye behavior in VR. However, the technology is still in it's infancy and it will take 3-5 years before we see it in consumer VR headsets.
<h5>Written by Shachar "Vice" Weis, founder and CEO of<a href="http://packet39.com">Packet39</a>, Virtual Reality training solutions for the manufacturing and power industries.</h5>
